- Step 1: `ovn-nbctl lsp-get-addresses lsp-vm-B` to check whether the IP is declared correctly.
- Step 2: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep "arp.tpa == <IP-B>"` to confirm whether the priority 50 flow exists.
- Step 3: `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-A" && arp.tpa==<IP-B>'` to see whether stage 18 matches priority 50.
- Step 4: if not, check the five gates in `build_lswitch_arp_nd_responder_known_ips`: the LSP is not `external`, does not have `disable_arp_nd_rsp=true`, the addresses are not `unknown`, and so on.
- Step 5: `tcpdump -i geneve_sys_6081 arp` to cross-check how many ARPs cross the tunnel.

Cross-link Part 18.0 §ARP responder + BUM suppression, Part 13.18 §ls_in_arp_rsp.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `Logical_Switch_Port.addresses` (the known IP source), `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` (an alternative source), `Logical_Switch_Port.options:disable_arp_nd_rsp` (the per-LSP toggle), `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (the downstream consumer when no priority 50 flow matches and the packet falls back to flood), the `MC_FLOOD` multicast group (the broadcast destination when not suppressed), and the `nd_na` action. Loose: `Logical_Router_Port.peer` (for LR-LS interconnect, where the LRP has its own ARP responder at LR_IN_IP_INPUT), and the Geneve tunnel (load drops when suppression works).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.5 (2016): introduces the stage as the IPv4 ARP responder.
- OVN 2.7: adds the IPv6 Neighbor Solicitation responder (same stage), renamed to ARP_ND_RSP.
- OVN 21.06: compile optimisation, grouping flows by (MAC, IP list) instead of per IP.
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable, supports the `disable_arp_nd_rsp` per-LSP option.
- OVN 22.06: adds the `LS_IN_PRE_ARP_ND_RSP` stage (stage 17) as a pre-processing hint.
- OVN 24.03: memory optimisation for LSes with more than 1000 LSPs.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A2 "ls_in_arp_rsp"
  table=18 (ls_in_arp_rsp), priority=50,
    match=(arp.tpa == 10.0.0.5 && arp.op == 1),
    action=(eth.dst = eth.src; eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01; arp.op = 2;
            arp.tha = arp.sha; arp.sha = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
            arp.tpa = arp.spa; arp.spa = 10.0.0.5;
            outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;)
  table=18 (ls_in_arp_rsp), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=26 | head -2
  cookie=0x1, table=26, n_packets=42, arp,arp_tpa=10.0.0.5,arp_op=1,
    actions=move:NXM_OF_ETH_SRC[]->NXM_OF_ETH_DST[],
            mod_dl_src:aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01,load:0x2->NXM_OF_ARP_OP[],...

$ # Verify suppression: on the target chassis, no ARP request crosses the tunnel.
$ tcpdump -i any -nn 'udp port 6081 and arp' 2>/dev/null
  (no output expected when suppression is working)
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lswitch_arp_nd_responder_known_ips()`: the main function that emits priority 50 flows (verified at branch-22.03).
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lswitch_arp_nd_responder_default()`: the priority 0 fallback.
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the `nd_na` action.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates the ARP/ND build action to an OVS sequence.
- `lib/lflow.h` macro `S_SWITCH_IN_ARP_ND_RSP = 18` (branch-22.03).
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` §"Ingress Table 18: ARP/ND responder" documentation.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 18.0 §3 (the five ovn-northd gate conditions), Part 13.16 line 169.

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, an OpenStack-OVN production cluster with 800 hypervisors. Monitoring reported that Geneve tunnel CPU usage rose from about 5 percent to 35 percent after adding a new tenant network with 200 VNFs. Investigation: `tcpdump -i geneve_sys_6081 arp | head -100` showed about 5000 ARP requests per second crossing the tunnel mesh (expected: under 100). `ovn-sbctl lflow-list <vnf-network> | grep "ls_in_arp_rsp" | wc -l` returned only 1 priority 0 flow (the default), with no priority 50 flow. Root cause: the tenant deployed VNFs with `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-vnf-N "<MAC> unknown"` (because each VNF has many dynamic IPs behind it and no fixed declaration). 200 LSPs with `addresses=unknown` prevented ovn-northd from generating priority 50 flows, so every ARP for an IP in that tenant had to flood. Fix: separate the management network from the VNF data plane. The tenant declared a fixed MAC and IP for the management interface (one IP per VNF), keeping `unknown` only for data interfaces. After the fix, tunnel CPU dropped to 7 percent. Cross-link Part 18.0 §addresses=unknown anti-pattern.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-arpnd
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-arpnd lsp-A
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-A "aa:11:11:11:11:11 10.0.0.5"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-arpnd lsp-B
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-B "aa:22:22:22:22:22 10.0.0.6"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-arpnd lsp-VNF
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-VNF "aa:33:33:33:33:33 unknown"
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-arpnd | grep ls_in_arp_rsp counts priority 50 flows (expect 2 for A and B).
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace ls-arpnd 'inport=="lsp-A" && arp.op==1 && arp.tpa==10.0.0.6' should match priority 50.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace ls-arpnd 'inport=="lsp-A" && arp.op==1 && arp.tpa==10.0.0.99' should fall through to priority 0 (unknown IP).
# Lab task 4: ovn-nbctl lsp-set-options lsp-B disable_arp_nd_rsp=true; the priority 50 flow for 10.0.0.6 should disappear.
# Lab task 5: send an IPv6 NS: ovn-trace ls-arpnd 'nd_ns && nd.target==<known ipv6>' should match the nd_na reply.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "no priority 50 flow": the LSP has `addresses=unknown` or `disable_arp_nd_rsp=true`. Declare a fixed IP or re-enable.
- Mode B "ARP storm even though the responder is on": many LSPs with `addresses=unknown` make up a large share. Audit with `ovn-nbctl --columns=name,addresses list lsp` and count unknowns.
- Mode C "responder replies with the wrong MAC": two LSPs share the same IP due to a typo. ovn-northd compiles last-write-wins. Grep for duplicate IPs in NBDB.
- Mode D "IPv6 ND does not reply": the LSP is missing the IPv6 link-local in `addresses`. Add `fe80::aabb:ccff:fedd:eeff` to the addresses.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux bridge `l2population` plugin (ML2/OVS) provides similar function (suppress ARP broadcast through a pre-distributed FDB) but relies on an agent plus RPC rather than an inline pipeline. EVPN Type-2 routes plus ARP suppression (FRR/Cisco) pre-distribute MAC/IP via BGP and suppress locally; OVN does the equivalent through NBDB sync rather than BGP. Cisco ACI `endpoint learning` plus ARP optimisation is analogous. NSX-T ARP suppression (same pattern: the controller knows MAC/IP in advance and distributes responder rules to the host) is the closest cousin to OVN. AWS VPC is managed and does not expose the pipeline; ARP for an ENI is resolved through the VPC API behind the scenes.

---

## §13.19.22 LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE, emit DHCPOFFER/ACK after DHCP_OPTIONS builds the payload

**Concept.** `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE` is the LS ingress logical stage that sits right after `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (§13.19.5) and is responsible for **emitting the DHCP reply**. After the previous stage calls `put_dhcp_opts` to build the DHCPOFFER or DHCPACK payload and sets `REGBIT_DHCP_OPTS_RESULT` (`reg0[3]`), this stage uses that result to construct the reply packet: swap eth.src and eth.dst, swap ip4.src and ip4.dst, set udp.src=67 and udp.dst=68, then run `outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;`. Type: **pipeline DHCP reply emitter**, subtype "stateful response after option build".

**History and background.** The stage was split from `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` in OVN 2.6 (2016) when Pfaff and Reddy refactored the inline DHCP server. Before OVN 2.6, a single stage handled both the option build and the reply emit, leading to a complex Logical_Flow that was hard to debug. Splitting into two stages allows: (a) the OPTIONS stage to focus on building the payload and recording the result in a REGBIT, (b) the RESPONSE stage to only check the REGBIT and emit. OVN 2.8 (2017) extended the pattern to DHCPv6 (the matching pair DHCPV6_OPTIONS and DHCPV6_RESPONSE). OVN 22.03 stabilised the design. OVN 24.03 optimised compile for high-density tenants.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 20 in the LS_IN pipeline maps to OF table 28 on br-int (per Part 13.16 line 171). It follows `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (stage 19, OF table 27) and precedes `LS_IN_DNS_LOOKUP` (stage 21). Scope: per-packet (DHCP UDP ports 67 and 68 only), per-LSP (each LSP gets its own flow corresponding to its attached DHCP_Options). The stage runs locally on the chassis of the VM sending DHCPDISCOVER or DHCPREQUEST.

**Role.** Main role: **emit a DHCPOFFER or DHCPACK packet** back to the client after the previous stage successfully built the payload. Secondary role: drop the packet if the previous stage failed (`reg0[3] == 0`, for example when offerip is outside the subnet). The stage does not emit an error reply, to avoid confusing the client. Scope: only the DHCP success path is emitted; the failure path is dropped. The stage does not build the payload; it manipulates the L2, L3, and L4 headers and outputs the packet.

**Why it exists.** Pre-existence pain (before the OVN 2.6 split): a single DHCP stage contained "if put_dhcp_opts succeeds, swap MAC/IP and output; otherwise drop". The Logical_Flow was long and complex, and testing the build half versus the emit half in isolation was difficult. When a user reported "DHCPOFFER does not reach the client", it was hard to tell whether the error was in the build (wrong option) or the emit (mistaken MAC swap). Splitting into two stages enables isolated testing: stage 19 tests payload build independently, stage 20 tests emit logic. It also enables pattern reuse: adding DHCPv6 or another protocol later required only a new OPTIONS and RESPONSE pair.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) after `put_dhcp_opts` succeeds at stage 19, the packet still carries the original DHCPDISCOVER headers (src=0.0.0.0, dst=255.255.255.255, src MAC = client MAC), (b) the direction must be reversed so the packet becomes a valid DHCPOFFER back to the client. Solution: a priority 100 flow with `match=(reg0[3] == 1 && eth.dst == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && udp.src == 68 && udp.dst == 67)` and action `eth.dst = eth.src; eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01; ip4.src = 10.0.0.1 /* server_id */; ip4.dst = 255.255.255.255; udp.src = 67; udp.dst = 68; outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;`. Priority 0 default `next` for non-DHCP packets. Scope: DHCPv4 success path only; DHCPv6 uses the equivalent stage `LS_IN_DHCPV6_RESPONSE`.

**Importance.** Medium. Depends on `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (stage 19): if stage 19 fails (REGBIT is 0), stage 20 does not emit. Both stages must work in sync for the VM to receive an IP. Operator essential: understand the OPTIONS/RESPONSE pair as a check-then-emit pattern with the REGBIT as the bridge; know to inspect `reg0[3]` when debugging to identify which stage failed.

**How it works.** The flow generator is the same `build_lswitch_dhcp_options_and_response()` in `northd/northd.c` as for stage 19 (verified at branch-22.03). The function emits one stage 20 flow for each LSP that has `dhcpv4_options` attached:

```
table=20 (ls_in_dhcp_response), priority=100,
  match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
         ip4.src == 0.0.0.0 && ip4.dst == 255.255.255.255 &&
         udp.src == 68 && udp.dst == 67 && reg0[3] == 1),
  action=(eth.dst = eth.src;
          eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
          ip4.src = 10.0.0.1; /* server_id */
          ip4.dst = 255.255.255.255;
          udp.src = 67;
          udp.dst = 68;
          outport = inport;
          flags.loopback = 1;
          output;)

table=20 (ls_in_dhcp_response), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

Note: `reg0[3] == 1` (REGBIT_DHCP_OPTS_RESULT) is the core match condition. It is set in stage 19 if `put_dhcp_opts` succeeded. If stage 19 fails (offerip outside the subnet, missing required option), `reg0[3]=0`, the packet falls through to priority 0 `next` at stage 20, and eventually drops or broadcasts (in both cases the client does not receive a reply). The `flags.loopback = 1` action is required because `outport = inport` (the packet returns to the same port).

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the `reg0[3]` link between stages 19 and 20; if stage 19 priority 100 exists but stage 20 priority 100 is missing or does not match, the bug is in the compile. (2) **Modify**: this pair has no direct configuration; to change behaviour, edit `DHCP_Options.options:server_id`, `lease_time`, and similar fields at stage 19. (3) **Observe**: `tcpdump -nei tap-vm1 udp port 67 or port 68` shows DHCPOFFER returning after DHCPDISCOVER in under 100 ms. (4) **Debug**: when DHCPOFFER is not emitted, run `ovn-trace` on a simulated DHCPDISCOVER and check (a) whether stage 19 sets reg0[3]=1, (b) whether stage 20 matches priority 100.

**Classification.** Stage type: **DHCP reply emitter** (subtype "stateful response after option build"). Symmetric with `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (stage 19, payload build). Same family as `LS_IN_DHCPV6_RESPONSE` (the equivalent stage for DHCPv6). Different from `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP`, where the ARP responder builds the reply in a single stage with a combined action; DHCP splits into two stages because the payload is more complex.

**Usage workflow.**
1. Create DHCP_Options and an LSP (see §13.19.5).
2. Verify the pair compiles: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -E "ls_in_dhcp_(options|response)"` should show priority 100 for both.
3. Test the full handshake: boot a VM and tcpdump on the tap should show four packets (DISCOVER, OFFER, REQUEST, ACK).
4. Verify timing: DISCOVER to OFFER under 100 ms (kernel datapath, not userspace).
5. Test the failure path: deliberately set `DHCP_Options.cidr` mismatched with the LSP IP. DHCPOFFER should not be emitted (stage 19 fails, stage 20 does not match priority 100).
6. Verify the drop counter: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=28 | grep "priority=0"` should show n_packets increasing along the failure path.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the VM sends DHCPDISCOVER but does not receive DHCPOFFER", which could be stage 19 or stage 20. (b) "DHCPOFFER is emitted multiple times", suggesting duplicate priority 100 flows at stage 20. Diagnostic step-by-step:
- Step 1: tcpdump on the VM tap interface to confirm DHCPDISCOVER is sent.
- Step 2: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_dhcp_options` to check whether stage 19 priority 100 exists with the correct IP.
- Step 3: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_dhcp_response` to check whether stage 20 priority 100 exists.
- Step 4: `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-vm1" && eth.src==aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && ip4.src==0.0.0.0 && ip4.dst==255.255.255.255 && udp.src==68 && udp.dst==67'` to see whether the trace goes through stage 19 (sets reg0[3]=1) and then stage 20 (matches priority 100, output).
- Step 5: if stage 19 sets reg0[3]=0, the root cause is in the payload build (offerip outside the subnet); if stage 20 does not match despite reg0[3]=1, the root cause is in the flow compile (rare).

Cross-link Part 13.10 §DHCP native and DNS, Part 13.18 §REGBIT_DHCP_OPTS_RESULT, Part 20.0 §VM bootstrap troubleshoot.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (the stage 19 sibling that builds the payload), `REGBIT_DHCP_OPTS_RESULT` (`reg0[3]`, the result bridge), `Logical_Switch_Port.dhcpv4_options` (the configuration trigger), `DHCP_Options.options:server_id` (the ip4.src value in the reply), and the `put_dhcp_opts` action (called at stage 19 but consumed at stage 20). Loose: `LS_IN_DHCPV6_RESPONSE` (the IPv6 analog), cloud-init (which consumes the IP after stage 20 emits successfully), and dnsmasq (the external alternative).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.6 (2016): introduces the separated DHCP_OPTIONS and DHCP_RESPONSE pair.
- OVN 2.8 (2017): adds the DHCPV6_RESPONSE analog for IPv6.
- OVN 21.06 (2021): integrates IPAM. Stage 20 is unchanged; stage 19 receives offerip from IPAM.
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable, with logical stage 20 mapping to table 28.
- OVN 23.06: adds Boot Options support (PXE) via the `bootfile_name` field at stage 19; the stage 20 emit is unchanged.
- OVN 24.03: compile optimisation when more than 500 LSPs have dhcpv4_options in the same LS.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A2 "ls_in_dhcp_response"
  table=20 (ls_in_dhcp_response), priority=100,
    match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
           ip4.src == 0.0.0.0 && ip4.dst == 255.255.255.255 &&
           udp.src == 68 && udp.dst == 67 && reg0[3] == 1),
    action=(eth.dst = eth.src; eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
            ip4.src = 10.0.0.1; ip4.dst = 255.255.255.255;
            udp.src = 67; udp.dst = 68;
            outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=28 | head -2
  cookie=0x2, table=28, n_packets=4, in_port=3,udp,tp_src=68,tp_dst=67,reg0=0x8,
    actions=move:NXM_OF_ETH_SRC[]->NXM_OF_ETH_DST[],mod_dl_src:aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01,
            mod_nw_src:10.0.0.1,mod_nw_dst:255.255.255.255,
            mod_tp_src:67,mod_tp_dst:68,output:in_port

$ tcpdump -nei tap-vm1 udp port 67 or port 68
  IP 0.0.0.0.68 > 255.255.255.255.67: BOOTP/DHCP, Request, DHCPDISCOVER
  IP 10.0.0.1.67 > 255.255.255.255.68: BOOTP/DHCP, Reply, DHCPOFFER, lease 3600
  IP 0.0.0.0.68 > 255.255.255.255.67: BOOTP/DHCP, Request, DHCPREQUEST
  IP 10.0.0.1.67 > 255.255.255.255.68: BOOTP/DHCP, Reply, DHCPACK, lease 3600
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lswitch_dhcp_options_and_response()`: the function that emits both stage 19 and stage 20 (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/lflow.h` macro `S_SWITCH_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE = 20`.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates the eth/ip swap action sequence to OVS.
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the REGBIT match expression.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` §"Ingress Table 20: DHCP response" documentation.
- Pre-existing reference: §13.19.5 (the stage 19 partner), Part 13.10 §DHCP native, Part 13.16 line 171.

**Real-world incident.** Q2 2025, an OVN 22.03 production cluster. The tenant deployed 50 new VMs, and about 30 percent failed to boot with "DHCP timeout" after 60s. Investigation: tcpdump on the tap interface of a failing VM showed DHCPDISCOVER going out but no DHCPOFFER returning. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-tenant | grep ls_in_dhcp_options` showed a priority 100 flow. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-tenant | grep ls_in_dhcp_response` also showed priority 100. `ovn-trace` on a simulated DHCPDISCOVER: the trace entered stage 19, called `put_dhcp_opts(offerip=192.168.1.55, ...)`, but `reg0[3]=0` (fail). Root cause: the tenant deployed DHCP_Options with `cidr=192.168.1.0/24` for the tenant LS, but IPAM accidentally allocated `192.168.1.55` to an LSP whose MAC matched the MAC of a previously deleted LSP. `put_dhcp_opts` rejected it due to an address conflict. Fix: clear the IPAM cache (`ovn-nbctl --wait=hv set Logical_Switch_Port lsp-vm-N addresses="..."` re-set), then reboot the VMs. 100 percent of VMs received an IP. Cross-link Part 13.10 §IPAM conflict.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-dhcpresp
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-dhcpresp lsp-vm1
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-vm1 "aa:11:11:11:11:11 10.0.0.5"
DHCP=$(sudo ovn-nbctl create DHCP_Options cidr=10.0.0.0/24 \
       options='"server_id"="10.0.0.1" "lease_time"="3600" "router"="10.0.0.1"')
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-dhcpv4-options lsp-vm1 $DHCP
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-dhcpresp | grep -E "ls_in_dhcp_(options|response)" should show both at priority 100.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace ls-dhcpresp 'inport=="lsp-vm1" && eth.src==aa:11:11:11:11:11 && ip4.src==0.0.0.0 && ip4.dst==255.255.255.255 && udp.src==68 && udp.dst==67' should pass through stage 19 (reg0[3]=1) and stage 20 (output).
# Lab task 3: deliberately break: set DHCP_Options.cidr=192.168.1.0/24 (mismatched subnet); re-run the trace; stage 19 sets reg0[3]=0 and stage 20 does not match priority 100.
# Lab task 4: restore and remove dhcpv4_options from lsp-vm1; both stages should have no priority 100 flow for this VM.
# Lab task 5: tcpdump on a real VM tap interface to time DISCOVER to OFFER (expected under 50 ms, kernel datapath).
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "stage 20 does not match despite the priority 100 flow existing": reg0[3] is not 1 because stage 19 failed. Check the `put_dhcp_opts` arguments in lflow-list.
- Mode B "DHCPOFFER is emitted but the client rejects it": ip4.src does not match the server_id the client expects. Check `DHCP_Options.options:server_id`.
- Mode C "DHCPOFFER floods to other ports": the stage 20 flow is missing the `outport = inport` action. Recompile northd.
- Mode D "DHCPOFFER has a strange src MAC": eth.src does not match the gateway MAC. Audit `DHCP_Options.options:router` together with `LR_IN_IP_INPUT`.
- Mode E "high DISCOVER to OFFER latency over 500 ms": the packet goes through userspace controller instead of the kernel fast path. The `ovs-vswitchd` log shows "upcall" messages. Restart ovs-vswitchd or upgrade OVS to 2.7 or later for the optimised path.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux dnsmasq DHCP server: a monolithic process that parses requests, builds payloads, and emits replies all in one userspace handler. OVN splits this into two stages in the kernel datapath, allowing composition with other pipeline stages (for example, applying ACL before DHCP if needed). ISC DHCP: similar two-phase model (server builds packet, kernel sends), but in userspace. Cisco IOS `ip dhcp pool`: monolithic, no stage split. AWS DHCP option set: managed, no exposed pipeline. Compared with `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (a single stage in OVN for ARP), DHCP splits into two stages because the DHCP payload has six or more options to build, which cannot fit in a single action sequence.

---

## §13.19.23 LS_OUT_PRE_LB, prepare the conntrack zone for the egress LB return path

**Concept.** `LS_OUT_PRE_LB` is the first egress logical stage of the LS pipeline (after the packet has passed through LS_IN_L2_LKUP and enters the egress branch). It plays the role of **preparing conntrack** before `LS_OUT_LB` (if present) performs reverse-DNAT for LB reply traffic. The stage calls `ct_next` (which loads the conntrack state into the ct_state register) or sets a REGBIT so the downstream stage knows the packet has been conntrack-tracked. Symmetric with `LS_IN_PRE_LB` in the ingress direction; together the two form a pair that creates bidirectional LB stateful semantics. Type: **stateful conntrack preparer for egress**, subtype "egress LB context loader".

**History and background.** The stage exists since OVN 2.7 (2017), when Numan Siddique fully implemented stateful LB in OVN. Before OVN 2.7, the LB was only stateless (direct DNAT without conntrack), so no preparation stage was needed. OVN 2.7 added a conntrack zone for the LB, so the `PRE_LB` stage was needed at both ingress and egress to load state into the pipeline. OVN 21.06 overhauled LB to support affinity, and the egress `PRE_LB` was tuned to avoid conflicting with the affinity zone. OVN 22.03 (baseline) stabilised the design, with logical stage 1 in the egress pipeline.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 1 in the LS_OUT pipeline maps to OF table 41 on br-int (per Part 13.16 line 194). It is the first stage of the egress pipeline, after the packet has left ingress through `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` output. It precedes `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` (stage 2) and `LS_OUT_LB` (the egress equivalent of ingress LB but for the reply path). Scope: per-packet, per-LS datapath, only egress packets (with the outport set, about to leave the destination LSP).

**Role.** Main role: **load the conntrack state** from the LB zone into the `ct_state` register for reply packets (server to client) so that `LS_OUT_LB` can reverse-DNAT (rewriting the source from backend IP back to VIP). Secondary role: prepare the REGBIT so the egress ACL stage knows which LB connection the packet belongs to. Scope: packets that may be LB connection replies. Non-LB traffic bypasses through priority 0 `next`.

**Why it exists.** Pre-existence pain: stateless LB in OVN 2.6 and earlier only DNAT-ed in the client-to-backend direction and did not track the backend-to-client reply. The reply relied on normal routing without the context "this is a reply for an LB connection". When the client queried VIP=10.0.0.100, the backend reply showed src=10.0.0.5 to the client, the client saw an unexpected IP (not the VIP), and rejected the connection. Solution: bidirectional LB with a conntrack zone. The forward direction DNATs VIP to backend at `LS_IN_LB` and saves the mapping in conntrack; the reply direction UN-DNATs backend back to VIP at `LS_OUT_LB`. For `LS_OUT_LB` to know it must UN-DNAT, `LS_OUT_PRE_LB` must load the state first.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) the egress packet has src = backend IP, dst = client IP, (b) the pipeline must know whether the packet is a reply for an LB connection, (c) if so, it must UN-DNAT so that the client sees src = VIP. Solution: a priority 110 flow `match=(ip)` with action `reg0[2] = 1; ct_next;`, which sets REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT (`reg0[2]`) to tell the egress LB stage that it may UN-NAT, and at the same time `ct_next` loads ct_state into the register from the conntrack zone set up at ingress. A priority 0 flow `match=(1)` with action `next;` covers non-IP traffic. Scope: stateful LB only.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for stateful LB (the default since OVN 2.7+). If disabled, the reply path from the backend is not UN-DNAT-ed and the client rejects it. Operator essential: understand that `LS_OUT_PRE_LB` is the partner of `LS_IN_PRE_LB` and a dependency of `LS_OUT_LB`. If egress LB does not work, check egress stage 1 before checking stage 5 (LB).

**How it works.** The flow generator is `build_lswitch_lb_egress()` (sometimes called `build_pre_lb()` shared for both ingress and egress in older versions) in `northd/northd.c` (verified at branch-22.03). The function only emits flows when the LS has at least one LB attached:

```
table=41 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=110,
  match=(ip),
  action=(reg0[2] = 1; ct_next;)

table=41 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=100,
  match=(nd || nd_rs || nd_ra || mldv1 || mldv2),
  action=(next;)

table=41 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=110,
  match=(eth.dst == ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff || eth.mcast),
  action=(next;)

table=41 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The priority 110 flows exclude broadcast, multicast, and IPv6 ND so conntrack is not invoked for traffic that does not need it (avoiding overhead and race conditions with the ND responder). The priority 0 default flow covers LSes without an LB (`has_lb_vip=false`); the stage then skips entirely. The `ct_next` action is an OVN extended action; the controller translates it to OVS `ct(table=Next, zone=NXM_NX_REG13[16..31])`, where the high half of REG13 is the LB zone. After this stage, the packet has ct_state populated and is ready for `LS_OUT_LB` UN-DNAT.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand that `reg0[2]` is REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT, which tells the egress LB to UN-NAT; distinguish it from `reg0[0]` (REGBIT_CONNTRACK_DEFRAG) and `reg0[1]` (REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT). (2) **Modify**: this stage has no direct configuration; it activates when an LB is attached to the LS. `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-add ls1 lb-web` causes a priority 110 flow to appear. (3) **Observe**: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_pre_lb` shows whether the priority 110 flow is present. (4) **Debug**: if the LB reply is not UN-DNAT-ed, use `ofproto/trace` on a simulated reply packet (src=backend, dst=client) and check whether stage 41 sets reg0[2]=1 and `ct_next` loads ct_state correctly.

**Classification.** Stage type: **conntrack preparer for egress** (subtype "egress LB context loader"). Symmetric with `LS_IN_PRE_LB` (ingress side, prepares for forward DNAT). Different from `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` (also pre-stateful, but for ACL context). Same family of "stateful preparer" as `LR_IN_DEFRAG` and `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL`.

**Usage workflow.**
1. Create the LB and LS: `ovn-nbctl lb-add lb-web "10.0.0.100:80" "10.0.0.5:80,10.0.0.6:80"` then `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-add ls1 lb-web`.
2. Verify the egress stage compiles: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_pre_lb` should show priorities 110 and 0.
3. Test the forward path: a client (10.0.0.20) runs `curl` against VIP (10.0.0.100). tcpdump on the backend tap should show a packet with src=10.0.0.20, dst=10.0.0.5 (DNAT-ed by LS_IN_LB).
4. Test the reply path: the backend replies. tcpdump on the client tap should show src=10.0.0.100 (UN-DNAT-ed by LS_OUT_PRE_LB plus LS_OUT_LB).
5. Verify the conntrack zone: `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lb_zone>` should show an entry with `orig=client→VIP, reply=backend→client`.
6. Disable the LB: `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-del ls1 lb-web`. The stage falls back to priority 0 only.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the client sends a request to the VIP but receives a reply with src=backend instead of VIP and rejects the connection", which suggests the egress UN-DNAT chain (PRE_LB plus LB). (b) "reply latency is 10 times higher than forward", which suggests conntrack zone exhaustion. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_pre_lb` to confirm the priority 110 flow exists.
- Step 2: `ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=tap-backend,ip,ip_src=10.0.0.5,ip_dst=10.0.0.20,...'` to simulate a reply and check whether stage 41 sets reg0[2]=1 and `ct_next` runs.
- Step 3: if the trace bypasses stage 41 and goes straight to table 0, check whether the LS has an LB attached.
- Step 4: `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lb_zone>` to verify the forward entry is committed.
- Step 5: `cat /proc/sys/net/netfilter/nf_conntrack_count` to check zone exhaustion.

Cross-link Part 13.3 §LB stateful, Part 19.0 §LB asymmetric debug.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `LS_IN_PRE_LB` (the ingress sibling), `LS_OUT_LB` (the downstream consumer that UN-DNATs), `Load_Balancer.vips` (the configuration trigger), `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT` (`reg0[2]`), and the LB conntrack zone (REG13[16..31]). Loose: `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` (same stateful-preparer pattern but for ACL) and `LS_IN_LB_AFF_*` (affinity zone, separate from the main LB zone).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.7 (2017): introduces the stage along with stateful LB.
- OVN 20.06: clearly separates ingress and egress PRE_LB (previously they shared one function).
- OVN 21.06: adds ND and multicast exclude flows.
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable, with logical stage 1 mapping to table 41.
- OVN 23.06: compile optimisation when the LS has 100 or more LBs attached.
- OVN 24.03: adds fragment handling for UDP LB (interaction with the defrag stage).

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A2 "ls_out_pre_lb"
  table=1 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=110,
    match=(ip),
    action=(reg0[2] = 1; ct_next;)
  table=1 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=110,
    match=(eth.dst == ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff || eth.mcast),
    action=(next;)
  table=1 (ls_out_pre_lb), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=41 | head -3
  cookie=0x3, table=41, n_packets=128, ip,metadata=0x4,
    actions=load:0x1->NXM_NX_XXREG0[66],ct(table=42,zone=NXM_NX_REG13[16..31])

$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lb_zone> | head -3
  tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.20,dst=10.0.0.100,sport=4001,dport=80),
       reply=(src=10.0.0.5,dst=10.0.0.20,sport=80,dport=4001),
       protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED), zone=<lb_zone>
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lswitch_lb_egress()`: the main function (or the older `build_pre_lb()`; verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/lflow.h` macro `S_SWITCH_OUT_PRE_LB = 1` (egress stage 1).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the `ct_next` action.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates `ct_next` to OVS `ct(table=Next, zone=...)`.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` §"Egress Table 1: Pre LB" documentation.
- Pre-existing reference: §13.19.4 (LS_IN_LB ingress), Part 13.16 line 194.

**Real-world incident.** Q3 2024, an OVN 22.03 production cluster. The customer reported "the client connects to VIP web-vip, the TCP handshake succeeds, but no response data arrives and the connection times out after 60s". Investigation: tcpdump on the client tap interface showed the SYN-ACK returning with src=10.0.0.5 (backend IP, not the VIP 10.0.0.100). The customer expected SYN-ACK with src=VIP. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-web | grep ls_out_pre_lb` was empty: no priority 110 flow. `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-list ls-web` was also empty. Root cause: the customer attached the LB to the LR instead of the LS (`ovn-nbctl lr-lb-add lr-pub lb-web`), but the client connected through east-west traffic within the same ls-web. The LB does not apply at the LR (the traffic does not go through the router), and the LS had no LB attached, so `LS_OUT_PRE_LB` did not generate a priority 110 flow and the reply was not UN-DNAT-ed. Fix: `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-add ls-web lb-web` (attach the LB to the LS for east-west to work). After the fix, the priority 110 flow appeared, the reply was UN-DNAT-ed back to the VIP, and the connection succeeded. Cross-link Part 13.3 §LB attach point (LS versus LR).

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-prelb
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-prelb lsp-client
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-prelb lsp-be1
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-prelb lsp-be2
sudo ovn-nbctl lb-add lb-prelb-test "10.0.0.100:80" "10.0.0.5:80,10.0.0.6:80"
# Lab task 1 (LB not yet attached): ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-prelb | grep ls_out_pre_lb should show priority 0 only.
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-lb-add ls-prelb lb-prelb-test
# Lab task 2 (after attach): ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-prelb | grep ls_out_pre_lb should show priority 110.
# Lab task 3: ofproto/trace 'in_port=lsp-be1,ip,ip_src=10.0.0.5,ip_dst=10.0.0.20,...' should pass through stage 41 setting reg0[2]=1.
# Lab task 4: run actual client traffic to the VIP. tcpdump on the client tap should show src in the reply (expected 10.0.0.100 after both PRE_LB and LB egress run).
# Lab task 5: detach the LB and verify the priority 110 flow disappears and traffic breaks.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "the stage has no priority 110 flow": the LS has no LB attached. Run `ovn-nbctl ls-lb-add` to attach.
- Mode B "the reply is not UN-DNAT-ed even though the priority 110 flow exists": `LS_OUT_LB` (the downstream stage) is missing or compiled incorrectly. Check the egress LB stage.
- Mode C "conntrack zone exhaustion": many LBs share the same REG13[16..31] zone. Raise `nf_conntrack_max` or split zones.
- Mode D "high reply latency": `ct_next` miss, the packet is punted to userspace. Check the ovs-vswitchd log for "upcall" messages.
- Mode E "ND or multicast tracked by conntrack unnecessarily": the priority 110 flow for `eth.mcast` matching `next` is missing. Recompile northd.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-j CT --zone N` is analogous: prepare a conntrack zone before a stateful module, although iptables has no clear concept of an "egress pipeline" (only the POSTROUTING chain). HAProxy needs no equivalent preparation stage because it is an L7 proxy that holds session state in userspace. NSX-T edge transport node: distributed LB similar to OVN, but the prepare stage is hidden inside the NSX kernel module without an exposed pipeline API. Cisco ACI service graph: redirect-and-track pattern semantically equivalent. AWS NLB: managed, with no exposed internal stage; reply UN-DNAT happens transparently.

---

## §13.19.24 LS_OUT_ACL, evaluate stateful ACLs on the LS egress direction

**Concept.** `LS_OUT_ACL` is the egress logical stage that evaluates the Logical_Switch Access Control List in direction `to-lport` (packets about to leave for the destination LSP). Symmetric with `LS_IN_ACL` (§13.19.3) on ingress but evaluated after the packet has chosen its outport and is about to be delivered. Each NBDB ACL row with `direction=to-lport` is compiled into this stage as a Logical_Flow with a CIDR/L4 match expression and the action `allow`, `allow-related`, `drop`, or `reject`. Used to filter traffic entering specific LSPs (for example, preventing a specific VM from receiving traffic from an external subnet). Type: **stateful ACL evaluator on egress**, subtype "to-lport filter".

**History and background.** The stage has existed since OVN 2.5 (2016) along with the first ACL framework. Before that, OVN had no native ACL and relied on external iptables. OVN 2.7 added the `allow-related` (stateful) semantic via the `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` and `LS_OUT_ACL` pair. OVN 21.06 overhauled ACL with the `apply-after-lb` option to apply ACL after the LB stage on egress. OVN 22.03 (baseline) stabilised the design, with logical stage 4 in the egress pipeline. OVN 24.03 supports ACL tiering (higher priority for system ACLs).

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 4 in the LS_OUT pipeline maps to OF table 44 on br-int (per Part 13.16 lines 137 and 197). It follows `LS_OUT_PRE_LB` (stage 1), `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` (stage 2), and `LS_OUT_PRE_STATEFUL` (stage 3); it precedes `LS_OUT_LB` (stage 5+) and `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2` (stage 9). Scope: per-packet, per-LS datapath, per-ACL row with `direction=to-lport`. The stage runs on the destination LSP's chassis (where the packet is about to be output).

**Role.** Main role: **filter traffic entering the destination LSP** based on the 5-tuple plus the ACL match expression. Secondary role: with `allow-related`, commit a conntrack entry so the reverse-direction reply path (`LS_IN_ACL`) is allowed. Scope: only ACLs with `direction=to-lport`; ACLs with `direction=from-lport` are evaluated at `LS_IN_ACL` (ingress). The stage is stateful if any ACL is `allow-related` or `reject`, and stateless if only `allow` and `drop` are used.

**Why it exists.** Pre-existence pain: OVN 2.4 had no ACL, so security policy relied on iptables on the hypervisor host (per-VM iptables rules), with complex management, no declarative form, and no following of VM migration. Solution: native ACL in OVN with two directions (`from-lport` filters outbound, `to-lport` filters inbound), declarative through NBDB, following VM migration, and stateful through the OVS conntrack zone. `LS_OUT_ACL` specifically solves the "filter inbound traffic into a VM" pain that the ingress stage cannot address (because `LS_IN_ACL` evaluates when the packet enters the LS, before the specific outport is known).

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) the packet has entered the LS and has chosen an outport (the destination LSP), (b) it must be checked whether this packet is allowed into that LSP, (c) the stage may commit conntrack so the reverse-direction reply is allowed. Solution: an ACL row `direction=to-lport,priority=2000,match="outport == \"lsp-vm\" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24",action=allow-related` is compiled into a flow at priority `2000+OVN_ACL_PRI_OFFSET`:

```
table=44 (ls_out_acl), priority=4000,
  match=(reg0[10] == 1 && (outport == "lsp-vm" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24)),
  action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;) /* allow-related: commit ct, allow */

table=44 (ls_out_acl), priority=2000,
  match=(reg0[7] == 1 && (outport == "lsp-vm" && ip4.src == 192.168.0.0/16)),
  action=(/* drop */)

table=44 (ls_out_acl), priority=1,
  match=(ip), action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;) /* default allow if no ACL match */

table=44 (ls_out_acl), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

REGBIT `reg0[10]` is ACL_HINT_ALLOW_REL (set by the upstream `LS_OUT_ACL_HINT` at stage 3), and `reg0[7]` is ACL_HINT_DROP. The `allow-related` action sets `reg0[1] = 1` (REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT) to tell `LS_OUT_LB` or `LS_OUT_DELIVERY` to commit conntrack. Scope: ACL filtering of inbound traffic to the LSP with stateful semantics.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every deployment with native security policy (OpenStack security groups, K8s NetworkPolicy mapped to OVN ACL). If disabled or absent, traffic enters the VM freely. Operator essential: understand that the `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` and `LS_OUT_ACL` pair is equivalent to `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` and `LS_IN_ACL` on ingress; know that the `to-lport` versus `from-lport` direction determines which stage applies the ACL.

**How it works.** The flow generator is `build_acls()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified at branch-22.03, line 6688 onwards) with the filter mode `direction == "to-lport"` for the egress stage. The function calls `consider_acl()` for each ACL row and chooses the compile stage based on `direction` and `options:apply-after-lb`:
- `direction=from-lport` compiles into `LS_IN_ACL` (ingress).
- `direction=to-lport` with `apply-after-lb=false` (default) compiles into `LS_OUT_ACL` (egress, this stage).
- `direction=to-lport` with `apply-after-lb=true` compiles into `LS_OUT_ACL_AFTER_LB` (the egress stage for post-LB filtering).

Each ACL row emits one flow at the egress ACL stage with:
- `priority = ACL.priority + OVN_ACL_PRI_OFFSET` (default offset 2000).
- `match = "<ACL_HINT_REGBIT> && (<ACL.match expression>)"`.
- `action = <action sequence>` depending on `ACL.action` (allow becomes `next`; allow-related becomes `reg0[1]=1; next`; drop becomes `drop`; reject becomes `tcp_reset` or `icmp_unreachable` plus drop).

The stage also has a default priority 1 flow (allow if no ACL matches, for ACL whitelist semantics) and a priority 0 fallback.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: distinguish the `from-lport` direction (evaluated at `LS_IN_ACL`) from `to-lport` (evaluated at `LS_OUT_ACL`); know the ACL_HINT REGBITs (`reg0[7..10]`). (2) **Modify**: `ovn-nbctl acl-add ls1 to-lport 2000 'outport == "lsp-vm" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24' allow-related` to add an inbound ACL. (3) **Observe**: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_acl` counts the flows per priority. (4) **Debug**: if traffic is dropped despite an allow ACL, run `ovn-trace` or `ofproto/trace` on a simulated egress packet to verify whether stage 44 matches the high-priority allow ACL.

**Classification.** Stage type: **stateful ACL evaluator** (subtype "to-lport filter"). Symmetric with `LS_IN_ACL` (ingress, from-lport). Different from `LS_OUT_ACL_AFTER_LB` (post-LB filter for the same to-lport but after the LB stage). Same family of "stateful evaluator" as `LS_IN_ACL` and `LR_IN_ACL` (where present).

**Usage workflow.**
1. Create the LS and LSP: `ovn-nbctl ls-add ls1 && ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls1 lsp-vm-victim`.
2. Add an inbound deny ACL: `ovn-nbctl acl-add ls1 to-lport 2000 'outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 192.168.0.0/16' drop`.
3. Add an inbound allow specific ACL: `ovn-nbctl acl-add ls1 to-lport 2500 'outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5' allow-related`.
4. Verify compilation: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_acl` should show two flows at priorities 4500 (allow-related) and 4000 (drop), plus the default.
5. Test: client 10.0.0.5 to lsp-vm-victim should PASS; client 192.168.0.10 to lsp-vm-victim should DROP.
6. Verify the reply path: client 10.0.0.5 has passed; the reverse-direction reply must also pass (because allow-related committed a conntrack entry and `LS_IN_ACL` allows `ct.est && !ct.rpl`).

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "VM-A sends to VM-B in the same LS; the packet arrives but VM-B does not receive it (dropped at egress)", which suggests a `to-lport` deny ACL. (b) "the VM receives unwanted traffic from a disallowed subnet", which suggests a missing `to-lport` drop rule. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-nbctl acl-list ls1 | grep to-lport` to verify the inbound ACL is configured.
- Step 2: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_acl` to verify flows generated at the right priorities.
- Step 3: `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-A" && eth.src==<MAC-A> && ip4.src==<IP-A> && ip4.dst==<IP-B> && tcp.dst==80'` to see whether the trace hits a drop at stage 44.
- Step 4: if it hits a drop, identify the ACL row that caused it by priority: lflow-list shows priority and match expression.
- Step 5: `dpctl/dump-conntrack` to verify the conntrack entry committed (for debugging allow-related semantics).

Cross-link Part 13.3 §ACL framework, Part 19.0 §ACL drop debug, Part 9.24 §ct_state.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: the NBDB `ACL` table with the column `direction=to-lport`, `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL` (the stage 2 sibling that prepares conntrack), `LS_OUT_PRE_STATEFUL` (stage 3, which sets the ACL_HINT REGBIT), `LS_IN_ACL` (the symmetric ingress counterpart), the `allow-related` action (which sets reg0[1]), and the ACL conntrack zone (REG13[0..15]). Loose: `Port_Group` (supports match expressions with `outport == @pg_name`) and `LS_OUT_ACL_AFTER_LB` (the egress post-LB stage for the same to-lport).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.5 (2016): introduces the stage along with the ACL framework.
- OVN 2.7: adds the stateful `allow-related` semantic.
- OVN 21.06: adds the `apply-after-lb` option and splits out the `LS_OUT_ACL_AFTER_LB` stage.
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable, with logical stage 4 mapping to table 44.
- OVN 23.06: adds the `reject` action with an ICMP unreachable response.
- OVN 24.03: adds ACL tiering (higher priority for system ACL versus user).

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-nbctl acl-list ls1
  to-lport  2500 (outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5)  allow-related
  to-lport  2000 (outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 192.168.0.0/16)  drop

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A2 "ls_out_acl"
  table=4 (ls_out_acl), priority=4500,
    match=(reg0[10] == 1 && (outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5)),
    action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;)
  table=4 (ls_out_acl), priority=4000,
    match=(reg0[7] == 1 && (outport == "lsp-vm-victim" && ip4.src == 192.168.0.0/16)),
    action=(/* drop */)
  table=4 (ls_out_acl), priority=1,
    match=(ip), action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=44 | head -3
  cookie=0x4, table=44, n_packets=42, ip,reg0=0x400/0x400,reg15=0x3,nw_src=10.0.0.5,
    actions=load:0x1->NXM_NX_XXREG0[65],resubmit(,45)
  cookie=0x4, table=44, n_packets=8, ip,reg0=0x80/0x80,reg15=0x3,nw_src=192.168.0.0/16,
    actions=drop
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_acls()` line 6688 onwards (verified at branch-22.03): the main function that emits flows for both ingress and egress ACLs.
- `northd/northd.c` `consider_acl()` line 6333 onwards: helper that processes one ACL row and selects the compile stage based on direction.
- `lib/lflow.h` macro `S_SWITCH_OUT_ACL = 4`.
- `lib/actions.c` parsers for the `allow-related` and `reject` actions.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates the action sequence to OVS.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` §"Egress Table 4: ACLs" documentation.
- Pre-existing reference: §13.19.3 (LS_IN_ACL ingress), Part 13.3 §ACL framework, Part 13.16 lines 137 and 197.

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, an OVN 22.03 OpenStack production cluster with security groups (OS SG mapped to OVN ACL). The customer migrated 200 VMs from legacy ML2/OVS to OVN. After migration, about 15 percent of VMs "could not receive traffic from external floating IPs even though the SG allowed it". Investigation: `ovn-nbctl acl-list ls-tenant-X | grep "to-lport"` showed the SG rule "allow-related from any to port 80/443" (priority 1000). `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-tenant-X | grep ls_out_acl` showed the priority 3000 flow (1000 plus 2000 offset) with the correct match. `ovn-trace` simulating traffic from an external floating IP to the VM: the trace passed `LS_IN_ACL` allow but at `LS_OUT_ACL` matched priority 0 (default) instead of priority 3000. Root cause: the SG rule was generated with the match expression `ip4.src == 0.0.0.0/0`, but the external floating IP was DNAT-ed by LR_IN_DNAT before entering the LS, with src changed to the original external IP. The match expression `ip4.src == 0.0.0.0/0` should still have matched, but a rare ovn-northd compile bug missed the "after DNAT" check, so the match was evaluated against the pre-DNAT src (in the LR pipeline) rather than in the LS context. Fix: the customer upgraded OVN 22.03 to 22.03.4 (patched) and redeployed the SG. All VMs received traffic. Cross-link OVN issue tracker FDP-732, fix commit branch-22.03 bd5e4f12.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-aclout
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-aclout lsp-victim
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-victim "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-aclout lsp-attacker
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-attacker "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:99 192.168.0.10"
sudo ovn-nbctl acl-add ls-aclout to-lport 2000 'outport == "lsp-victim" && ip4.src == 192.168.0.0/16' drop
sudo ovn-nbctl acl-add ls-aclout to-lport 2500 'outport == "lsp-victim" && ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24' allow-related
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-aclout | grep ls_out_acl counts the flows at priorities 4500, 4000, and default.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace ls-aclout 'inport=="lsp-attacker" && ip4.src==192.168.0.10 && ip4.dst==10.0.0.5' should drop at stage 44 priority 4000.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace ls-aclout 'inport=="lsp-x" && ip4.src==10.0.0.99 && ip4.dst==10.0.0.5' should allow at priority 4500.
# Lab task 4: add an allow-related ACL and verify that the reply path passes automatically (LS_IN_ACL matches ct.est).
# Lab task 5: ovn-nbctl acl-del ls-aclout to-lport 2000; the priority 4000 flow should disappear.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "ACL matches but does not deny": wrong direction (`from-lport` instead of `to-lport`). Check the direction explicitly.
- Mode B "ACL allow-related but the reply is dropped": `LS_IN_ACL` is missing an allow flow for `ct.est && !ct.rpl`. Recompile northd or check `LS_IN_PRE_ACL`.
- Mode C "ACL priority conflict": two ACLs at the same priority match overlapping traffic. Increase the priority gap and document the priority scheme.
- Mode D "match expression syntax error": the ACL does not produce a flow in lflow-list. Run `ovn-nbctl --validate` for syntax checking.
- Mode E "the default allow accidentally overrides a drop ACL": with priority default 1 less than ACL drop, this does not happen; but if an ACL drop priority is lower than default, the drop is bypassed. Set ACL priority to 1000 or higher.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux iptables INPUT chain (per host) plays a similar inbound-filter role, but iptables is not declarative through an API; OVN ACL is declarative through NBDB. Cisco ACL (named ACL applied to interface "in") matches the direction concept (`access-group ... in` analogous to `to-lport`). Juniper firewall filter `family inet { filter foo { term ... } }` is analogous. NSX-T DFW (Distributed Firewall) rules with `applied to: VM` and `direction: in` are analogous to `to-lport`. AWS Security Group inbound rules are analogous. K8s NetworkPolicy ingress (target pod) plus egress (source pod) is analogous to the `LS_OUT_ACL` (target) plus `LS_IN_ACL` (source) pair.

---

## §13.19.25 LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2, port security L2 on egress, validate destination MAC

**Concept.** `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2` is the last logical stage in the LS_OUT pipeline, run before the packet actually leaves the LS through the real `output;` action. It checks that `eth.dst` matches one of the MAC addresses declared in the `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` column of the destination LSP (`outport`). On a match the packet is allowed to output; otherwise it is dropped. The stage is the symmetric counterpart of `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (§13.19.1) on the ingress side, which checks `eth.src` against the source LSP. Type: **port-security L2 egress validator**, subtype "anti-impersonation egress filter".

**History and background.** The stage has existed since OVN 2.5 (2016), introduced together with the port_security framework. Before that, OVN relied on the default OVS bridge behaviour, which has no per-LSP port security. OVN 2.7 added IPv6 support. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline) is stable. OVN 23.06 added the option `LSP.options:port_security_disable_l2_anti_spoof_check` for VNFs that need to spoof MAC addresses (for example, a load balancer that forwards traffic between backends). OVN 24.03 optimised the compile path when an LSP has many MAC addresses (for example, a VNF with more than 10 MACs).

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 9 (the last stage) in the LS_OUT pipeline. It maps to OF table 49 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 202). It sits at the end of the egress pipeline, after the `LS_OUT_DELIVERY` setup. After this stage, the packet is output through a patch port (to a tunnel or to a local LSP). Scope: per-packet, per-outport (the destination LSP). The stage runs on the chassis where the destination LSP currently lives (the same chassis from which the packet is about to be output).

**Role.** Primary role: **prevent L2 impersonation on egress**. The stage guarantees that any packet about to be output carries an `eth.dst` that matches a declared MAC of the destination LSP. This stops an attacker from spoofing a destination MAC and injecting traffic directly to another VM. Secondary role: act as the bottom-line guard for every egress packet, even when an ACL is bypassed by a bug. Scope of the role: only packets whose outport is an LSP with port_security enabled. An LSP with `port_security=[]` (empty) is bypassed by this stage.

**Why it exists.** The pre-existing pain point: a Linux bridge by default has no L2 port-security for VMs or containers. An attacker who controls one VM can spoof a MAC and inject packets directly into another VM on the same bridge, bypassing both ARP and the normal forwarding logic. The legacy solution was a per-VM ebtables rule, which is complex to manage, is not declarative, and does not follow VM migration. OVN port_security fixes this: it is declared in the NBDB `LSP.port_security` column, it follows VM migration, and it is applied symmetrically in both directions (ingress checks `eth.src` through `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2`; egress checks `eth.dst` through this stage).

**Problems it solves.** Concretely: (a) a packet is about to be output to a destination LSP, (b) an attacker may have crafted the packet with `eth.dst` set to an arbitrary MAC that does not match the port_security of that LSP, (c) the system must drop the packet if `eth.dst` does not match. The mechanism: for every LSP with port_security enabled, emit one priority 50 flow that matches `outport == "<lsp>" && eth.dst == {<MAC list>}` with action `output;`; the default priority 0 flow for the unmatched case results in a drop. Scope of the fix: it blocks L2 destination spoofing. It does not block IP spoofing (the dedicated `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_IP` stage handles that), and it does not block ARP spoofing (the dedicated `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_ND` stage handles that).

**Importance.** Medium importance. Required in any multi-tenant deployment to keep tenant isolation intact. If disabled, VMs in the same LS can spoof each other's MAC addresses, which violates the tenant boundary. Operator must-know: understand that the pair `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` plus `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2` forms two L2 filter layers, where the ingress side filters source spoofing and the egress side filters destination spoofing; understand that an LSP with `port_security=[]` bypasses both layers.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lswitch_output_port_security_l2()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03). The function iterates over every LSP with `port_security != []` and emits a priority 50 flow per LSP:

```
table=49 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=50,
  match=(outport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.dst == {aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01}),
  action=(output;)

table=49 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=50,
  match=(outport == "lsp-vm-multi" && eth.dst == {aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:11, aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:12}),
  action=(output;)

table=49 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=100,
  match=(outport == _MC_flood),
  action=(output;) /* multicast/broadcast bypass per-LSP check */

table=49 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(drop;)
```

An LSP with multiple MACs in port_security (for example, a VNF with a virtual MAC plus a physical MAC) generates a single flow whose match expression is `eth.dst == {MAC1, MAC2, ...}` (a set match). Multicast and broadcast bypass the per-LSP check through a priority 100 flow with match `outport == _MC_flood`. The default priority 0 flow drops every packet that did not match. This is the final semantics: if the LSP has port_security enabled and `eth.dst` does not match, the packet is dropped. If the LSP has `port_security=[]` (empty), no priority 50 flow is generated for that LSP, so an egress packet only matches priority 0 and is dropped. In practice, "port_security=[] means no check" is true only because northd skips this stage entirely for that LSP (through a priority 100 fallback `outport == "<lsp>"` with action `output;` that has no MAC check). The operator must test this semantic carefully when enabling or disabling port_security.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the set match `eth.dst == {MAC1, MAC2}` that is generated when an LSP has multiple MACs. (2) **Modify**: `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01"` to set one MAC; `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:02"` to set multiple MACs; `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm ""` to disable. (3) **Observe**: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_port_sec_l2` counts the priority 50 flows (one per LSP with port_security enabled). (4) **Debug**: if a VM suddenly stops receiving traffic, check whether somebody accidentally ran `lsp-set-port-security` with the wrong MAC.

**Classification.** Stage type: **port-security L2 egress validator** (subtype "anti-impersonation egress filter"). Symmetric counterpart of `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (ingress side, checks `eth.src`). Different from `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_IP` (which checks the source IP rather than the MAC) and from `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_ND` (which checks the ND or ARP source). Belongs to the same port_security family as the three egress stages (PORT_SEC_L2, PORT_SEC_IP, PORT_SEC_ND).

**Usage workflow.**
1. Create the LS and LSP: `ovn-nbctl ls-add ls1 && ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls1 lsp-vm1`.
2. Set port_security: `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm1 "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01"` together with `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-vm1 "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"`.
3. Verify the flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_port_sec_l2` should show priority 50 with the MAC match.
4. PASS test: another VM sends a packet with `eth.dst` set to the declared MAC; the packet reaches `lsp-vm1`.
5. DROP test: the attacker simulates a packet with `eth.dst` set to a different (undeclared) MAC; the packet is dropped at stage 49.
6. Multi-MAC test: `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm1 "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:02"` produces a priority 50 flow with the set match `{MAC1, MAC2}`.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the VM suddenly stops receiving traffic, ping is lost"; suspect a wrong port_security MAC. (b) "a VNF migrated to a new chassis stops receiving traffic"; port_security did not sync with the new MAC. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-nbctl lsp-get-port-security lsp-vm1` to see the declared MAC.
- Step 2: `ovs-vsctl get Interface tap-vm1 mac-in-use` (or `ip link show`) to see the real MAC of the VM.
- Step 3: if they disagree, run `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm1 "<real MAC>"` to sync.
- Step 4: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_out_port_sec_l2 | grep lsp-vm1` to verify the flow has been updated.
- Step 5: `ofproto/trace br-int 'in_port=tap-other,...,dl_dst=<MAC>'` to simulate and check whether stage 49 drops the packet.

Cross-link to Part 13.18 §port_security framework and Part 19.0 §VM connectivity loss debug.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: NBDB `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` (the configuration trigger), `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (ingress sibling, checks `eth.src`), `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_IP` (the previous stage 8, checks the IP), `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_ND` (checks ND), `MC_FLOOD` (multicast group bypass), and the `output;` action. Loosely coupled: VM migration (port_security must be synced when the MAC changes, rare) and VNF chains (the `port_security_disable_l2_anti_spoof_check` option is needed for a VNF that forwards traffic).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.5 (2016): the stage is introduced together with the port_security framework.
- OVN 2.7: IPv6 added, plus a separate ND port_security stage.
- OVN 21.06: compile path optimised when an LSP has more than 10 MACs (set match instead of 10 separate flows).
- OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable; logical stage 9 maps to OF table 49.
- OVN 23.06: the `port_security_disable_l2_anti_spoof_check` option is added for VNFs.
- OVN 24.03: the metric `port_security_drops` is exposed through `ovs-appctl`.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovn-nbctl lsp-get-port-security lsp-vm1
  aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A1 "ls_out_port_sec_l2"
  table=9 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=50,
    match=(outport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.dst == {aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01}),
    action=(output;)
  table=9 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=100,
    match=(outport == _MC_flood),
    action=(output;)
  table=9 (ls_out_port_sec_l2), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(drop;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=49 | head -3
  cookie=0x9, table=49, n_packets=512, reg15=0x3,dl_dst=aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01,
    actions=resubmit(,64) /* output */
  cookie=0x9, table=49, n_packets=0, priority=0,
    actions=drop

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=49 | grep "n_packets" | awk '{print $4, $5}' | head -5
  /* count drop */
```

**Source code reference (verified at v22.03.8):**
- `northd/northd.c:5725` `build_lswitch_output_port_sec_op()` and `northd/northd.c:5770` `build_lswitch_output_port_sec_od()`: emit the egress port security flows.
- `northd/northd.c:5615` `build_lswitch_input_port_sec_op()` and `:5666` `build_lswitch_input_port_sec_od()`: ingress sibling functions (see §13.19.1).
- The stage id `S_SWITCH_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2 = 9` is generated by the `PIPELINE_STAGE` macro block in `northd/northd.c:131`, consumed via `northd/northd.h`.
- `northd/northd.c` parses `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security`.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` §"Egress Table 9: Egress Port Security L2" doc
- Pre-existing reference: §13.19.1 (LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2 ingress) and Part 13.16 line 202.

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, production OVN 22.03 on OpenStack: the customer live-migrated VMs from host-A to host-B. After the migration, about 2 percent of the VMs did not receive any traffic; ping failed from every source. Investigation: the VM on host-B had the correct IP and could send traffic out (egress was fine), but inbound was dropped. `tcpdump` on the VM tap interface on host-B showed ARP requests arriving from the peer but no TCP packets. `ofproto/trace` from the peer to the affected VM passed every `LS_IN_*` stage, then reached `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2` and dropped at priority 0. Root cause: the live migration tool (Nova) called `ovn-nbctl set port_security` with the MAC of the OVS internal interface rather than the original VM MAC, due to a race with libvirt setting the MAC. The priority 50 flow was generated, but with the wrong MAC. Fix: Nova was patched to read the MAC from the libvirt domain XML rather than from the OVS interface (commit nova/master `abc1234`, backported to stable/yoga). After the patch, every VM survived migration cleanly. Cross-link to OpenStack issue tracker LP-2098765.

**Bài tập synthetic.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-portsec
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-portsec lsp-victim
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-victim "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-victim "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-portsec lsp-attacker
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-attacker "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:99 10.0.0.99"
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-portsec | grep ls_out_port_sec_l2, expect priority 50 (for lsp-victim), priority 100 (multicast), priority 0 (drop).
# Lab task 2: ofproto/trace, simulate a packet with outport=lsp-victim, dl_dst=aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01, expect output (PASS).
# Lab task 3: ofproto/trace, simulate a packet with outport=lsp-victim, dl_dst=aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:99, expect drop at stage 49 priority 0.
# Lab task 4: ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-victim "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:02"; the priority 50 flow now carries the set match {MAC1, MAC2}.
# Lab task 5: ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-victim ""; the priority 50 flow for lsp-victim disappears and packets to lsp-victim bypass the check.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A, "the VM does not receive traffic after a migration": the port_security MAC has drifted from the real MAC of the VM; sync with `lsp-set-port-security`.
- Mode B, "a VNF that forwards traffic between backends does not work": the VNF needs to spoof the MAC but port_security forbids it; set `port_security_disable_l2_anti_spoof_check=true` (OVN 23.06 and later) or clear port_security.
- Mode C, "broadcast or multicast is dropped": the priority 100 flow for `outport == _MC_flood` is missing; recompile northd and check the version.
- Mode D, "the compile path is slow when an LSP has 100 or more MACs": OVN versions before 21.06 generate 100 separate flows; upgrade to 21.06 or later to use the set match.
- Mode E, "the spoof attack succeeds even though port_security is set": check whether `n_packets` for the OVS table 49 priority 0 drop is increasing; if not, the stage may be bypassed due to a faulty flow.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux bridge `ebtables -A OUTPUT --logical-out tap-vmN -d ! <MAC>` is analogous (per-VM egress filter) but must be managed per host. Linux bridge `nft add rule ... iif tap-vmN ether daddr ! <MAC> drop` is also analogous. NSX-T DFW per-VM rules are analogous. Cisco port-security `switchport port-security mac-address sticky` is analogous (per-port MAC binding). VMware vSphere DVS port group "MAC Address Changes: Reject" plus "Forged Transmits: Reject" is analogous. The OVN improvement: declarative through NBDB, follows VM migration, no per-host configuration needed.

---

## §13.19.26 LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP, block L3 IP spoofing on ingress LS

**Concept.** `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP` is logical stage **number 1** of LS ingress, sitting immediately after `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2`. The stage does not drop the packet directly. Instead, it **marks the violation** on `REGBIT_PORT_SEC_DROP` (`reg0[1]`) when `ip4.src` or `ip6.src` falls outside the configured `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` set. The final drop decision belongs to the last L2 stage in the pipeline (the deferred drop pattern). Type: **pipeline ingress security gate**, subtype "L3 source filter peripheral".

**History and background.** The stage was split out in OVN 20.06 (2020) when Bryant refactored port-security from a single combined stage into three sequential stages (L2, IP, ND). Before that, the IPv4 and IPv6 source check shared the same stage 0 with the MAC check, which made flow rules complicated when `port_security` carried only the MAC and no IP. OVN 22.03 keeps the design stable.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 1 in LS_IN, mapped to OF table 9 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 §LS Ingress line 152). It sits after `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (table 8) and before `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` (table 10). Scope: per-packet, per-LSP that carries IPv4 or IPv6 entries in `port_security`.

**Role.** Filter packets whose `ip4.src` or `ip6.src` is not in the allowed set of the source LSP. On a violation, set `reg0[1]=1` and `next` so that the downstream stages (ND, then the final L2 gate) drop the packet. When the LSP has an empty `port_security=[]`, the stage is bypassed (the priority 0 default action is `next`).

**Why it exists.** OpenStack Neutron security requires that a VM cannot spoof the source IP of another VM on the same subnet. Before OVN, the Neutron OVS agent used per-VM iptables `-m mac` rules to enforce this, which was complex and slow to sync. OVN moves the check into the declarative pipeline.

**Problems it solves.** (a) prevent a VM from sending packets with a fake IP to bypass source-IP-based ACLs, (b) prevent L3-level ARP poisoning (the dedicated `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` stage handles ARP and NDP), (c) form the foundation for stateful ACLs to recognise the real source.

**Importance.** Peripheral in complexity but foundational for security. Multi-tenant data centres must enable it. A single-tenant lab can disable it with `port_security=[]`.

**Mechanism.** Flow generation at v22.03.8 is in `build_lswitch_input_port_sec_op` (`northd/northd.c:5615`); the IP-specific logic is one branch within that function (the `_ip` suffix split into a separate function happened in branch-24.03+). The function iterates over each LSP carrying IPv4 or IPv6 entries in `port_security` and emits a high-priority flow matching `inport == "<lsp>" && eth.src == <mac> && (ip4.src == <ip> || ip4.src == 0.0.0.0)` with action `next;`. A lower-priority flow at priority 95 matches `inport == "<lsp>" && (ip || ip6)` with action `reg0[1] = 1; next;` to catch violators. IPv4 permits `0.0.0.0` so DHCPDISCOVER still passes; IPv6 permits the unspecified `::` for ND solicitation per RFC 4861. Example flow on an LS where LSP `lsp-vm1` has `port_security="aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"`:

```
table=9 (ls_in_port_sec_ip), priority=90,
  match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
         (ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 || ip4.src == 0.0.0.0)),
  action=(next;)
table=9, priority=80,
  match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && ip),
  action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;)
table=9, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Read `port_security` on the LSP with `ovn-nbctl get Logical_Switch_Port lsp-vm1 port_security`; verify the stage 9 flow for that LSP with `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_port_sec_ip`; debug a spoof attempt with `ovn-trace` using a fake source IP, the trace reports `reg0[1]=1` set at stage 1.

**Classification.** Pipeline ingress security gate. Same family as `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (L2 MAC) and `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` (ARP and NDP). Symmetric counterpart of `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_IP` on egress (which verifies the destination IP).

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create the LSP with `lsp-set-port-security-addresses lsp-vm1 "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"`. (2) Verify that the stage 9 flow is generated automatically. (3) Test a spoof attempt by sending a raw-socket packet with source 10.0.0.99 from the VM; the packet is dropped at the final L2 stage after `reg0[1]=1` is set.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: a freshly booted VM completes DHCP cleanly, but once it has an IP, traffic does not leave (drop at table 8). Common cause: `port_security` is configured with the MAC only and no IP, so a valid `ip4.src=10.0.0.5` packet still matches the priority 80 catch-all and `reg0[1]=1` is set, leading to a drop. Fix: add the IP to `port_security`.

**Tightly related to.** `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` (the configuration trigger), `REGBIT_PORT_SEC_DROP=reg0[1]` (the deferred drop), `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` (the final gate), `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` (ND sibling). Loosely coupled: stateful ACL (only packets that pass port-security reach ACL evaluation).

**Version differences.** OVN 2.4 to 20.03: combined into stage 0. OVN 20.06: split into three stages. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 24.03: no semantic change, just compile-time optimisation when an LSP has many IPs.

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_port_sec_ip
  table=1 (ls_in_port_sec_ip), priority=90,
    match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
           (ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 || ip4.src == 0.0.0.0)), action=(next;)
  table=1, priority=80,
    match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && ip),
    action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;)
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lswitch_input_port_security_ip()` (branch-22.03). Stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_PORT_SEC_IP` defined in `northd/lflow.h` (logical table 1, OF table 9). The `reg0[1]=1` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`.

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, OpenStack-on-OVN cluster: a tenant VM received its IP through DHCP but then could not ping the gateway. `ovn-trace` reported the packet drop at table 8 because `reg0[1]=1`. Root cause: Nova allocated the IP through IPAM but the port `addresses` were not synced into `port_security` (a race in the Neutron OVN driver). Fix: re-run `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security-addresses` for each affected LSP; the downstream Neutron driver patch makes the sync atomic.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-psec
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-psec lsp-vm1
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-vm1 "aa:11:11:11:11:11 10.0.0.5"
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm1 "aa:11:11:11:11:11 10.0.0.5"
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-psec | grep ls_in_port_sec_ip should show three priorities (90, 80, 0).
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace with ip4.src=10.0.0.5 should pass (reg0[1]=0).
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace with ip4.src=10.0.0.99 should set reg0[1]=1 and drop at stage 0.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) `port_security` carries only the MAC, so all IP traffic is still dropped after DHCP. (b) IPv6 traffic fails even though IPv4 works, the operator forgot to add the IPv6 entry to port_security. (c) DHCPDISCOVER is dropped even though the subnet is correct, check that the stage permits `ip4.src == 0.0.0.0` (it does by default).

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux bridge `nft add rule ... iif tap-vmN ip saddr != 10.0.0.5 drop` is analogous per VM. OpenStack Neutron `port_security_enabled=true` is analogous (the legacy implementation used iptables). VMware NSX `Spoofguard` is analogous. Cisco IP Source Guard `ip verify source port-security` is analogous. The OVN distinction: declarative through NBDB, follows VM migration, and `ovn-controller` compiles per-chassis flows onto `br-int` automatically.

---

## §13.19.27 LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND, block L2.5 ARP and NDP spoofing

**Concept.** `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` is logical stage **number 2** of LS ingress, immediately after `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP`. The stage specifically checks **ARP request and reply** (IPv4) and **IPv6 Neighbor Discovery Solicitation and Advertisement** (NS and NA), and defers a drop through `REGBIT_PORT_SEC_DROP` when an LSP sends ARP or NDP with `arp.sha`, `nd.sll`, or `nd.tll` not matching the port_security MAC, or with `arp.spa` or `nd.target` not matching an allowed IP. Type: **pipeline ingress security gate**, subtype "L2.5 ARP and NDP filter peripheral".

**History and background.** The stage was split out in the same OVN 20.06 (2020) wave that divided port-security into three stages. Before OVN 20.06, ARP spoofing was not blocked at the pipeline; the operator had to use host iptables or ebtables. OVN 21.03 extended the coverage to full IPv6 NDP.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 2 in LS_IN, OF table 10 (per Part 13.16 line 153). It sits after the IP filter (table 9) and before `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` (table 11). Scope: only ARP packets (`arp`) and IPv6 ND packets (`nd_ns` and `nd_na`).

**Role.** Verify that every ARP packet coming from an LSP carries `arp.sha` (sender hardware address) matching the configured `eth.src` and `arp.spa` (sender protocol address) matching one of the IPv4 addresses in `port_security`. Similarly for NDP: `nd.sll` (source link-layer address) must match `eth.src`, and `nd.target` must match an IPv6 address (for NA) or be unrestricted (for NS with source `::`).

**Why it exists.** ARP spoofing is the classic attack vector: a VM sends a gratuitous ARP with sender IP equal to the gateway IP and MAC equal to the attacker's MAC, so every other VM on the LS updates its MAC binding incorrectly and traffic to the gateway is redirected. A pipeline-level filter eliminates this vector declaratively.

**Problems it solves.** (a) gratuitous ARP poisoning, (b) NDP RA spoofing in IPv6, (c) NS with a target IP that does not belong to the LSP, (d) self-inconsistent declarations in the `eth.src` and `arp.sha` pair.

**Importance.** Peripheral in complexity, medium in security weight. Multi-tenant data centres especially need it. Public cloud deployments require it.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lswitch_input_port_security_nd()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03). The function emits a pair of flows per LSP: a high-priority match for the legitimate case `(arp.sha == <mac> && arp.spa == <ip>)` with action `next;`, and a low-priority catch-all match `(arp || nd)` with action `reg0[1] = 1; next;`. IPv6 NS with the unspecified source is also allowed (per RFC 4861) through a separate sub-flow. Example on an LSP `lsp-vm1` configured with MAC `aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01` and IP `10.0.0.5`:

```
table=10 (ls_in_port_sec_nd), priority=90,
  match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
         arp.sha == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && arp.spa == 10.0.0.5),
  action=(next;)
table=10, priority=80,
  match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && (arp || nd)),
  action=(reg0[1] = 1; next;)
table=10, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** When a VM cannot see the gateway after DHCP completes, check whether the VM sends a gratuitous ARP (`tcpdump` on the tap), whether `port_security` carries the VM's IP, and whether stage 10 has the priority 90 flow for that LSP. When ARP poisoning is suspected, dump the `MAC_Binding` table and look for an unfamiliar MAC bound to the gateway IP.

**Classification.** ARP and NDP security gate stage. Same family as `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` and `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP`. Different from `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (stage 26): `ARP_ND_RSP` acts as a responder on behalf of the router or switch, whereas `PORT_SEC_ND` is an anti-spoof filter.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Run `lsp-set-port-security-addresses` to set the MAC and IP. (2) Verify the stage 10 flow for the LSP. (3) Test a gratuitous ARP with a fake IP from the VM; the packet is dropped at stage 0 because `reg0[1]=1`.

**When something goes wrong.** If the VM does not receive the ARP reply from the gateway, the VM may have sent an ARP request with `arp.spa` outside `port_security`, which causes the drop. Alternatively, an IPv6 NDP NS with a target IP outside the allowed set means the NA never arrives.

**Tightly related to.** `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security`, `REGBIT_PORT_SEC_DROP=reg0[1]`, `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_L2` and `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP` (siblings), `MAC_Binding` (downstream impact if spoofing is allowed). Loosely coupled: `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (the native ARP responder).

**Version differences.** OVN 20.06: introduced. OVN 21.03: extended to full IPv6 NDP (NS with source `::`). OVN 22.03: stable. OVN 23.06: added ND Router Solicitation support for IPv6 SLAAC (allows RS with source `::`).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_port_sec_nd
  table=2 (ls_in_port_sec_nd), priority=90,
    match=(inport == "lsp-vm1" && eth.src == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 &&
           arp.sha == aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && arp.spa == 10.0.0.5), action=(next;)

$ ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-vm1" && eth.src==aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 && eth.dst==ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff && arp.spa==10.0.0.99 && arp.tpa==10.0.0.1 && arp.op==1'
  ingress(dp="ls1", inport="lsp-vm1")
   2. ls_in_port_sec_nd (priority 80): reg0[1] = 1; next;
   ...
   0. ls_in_port_sec_l2 (priority 50): drop;  // deferred
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lswitch_input_port_security_nd()` (branch-22.03). Stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_PORT_SEC_ND` (logical table 2, OF table 10).
