The NB schema `Logical_Switch_Port.port_security` is documented in `ovn-nb.xml` under port_security (verified: IPv6 ND solicitation allows the unspecified address).

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, OVN 22.03 deployment: a pen-tester ran `arpspoof` from a tenant VM to impersonate the gateway IP. In a normal Linux bridge setup ARP poisoning would have succeeded immediately, but OVN dropped every ARP packet with the wrong `arp.spa`. The pen-test report: the cluster passed the ARP poisoning test. Verified through `ovn-trace`, which showed `reg0[1]=1`.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-port-security lsp-vm1 "aa:11:11:11:11:11 10.0.0.5"
# Lab task 1: ovn-trace gratuitous ARP with arp.spa==10.0.0.5 should pass.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace with arp.spa==10.0.0.99 should set reg0[1]=1 and drop.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace IPv6 NS with nd.target==fe80::1, src=:: should pass (unspecified allowed).
# Lab task 4: arping -U 10.0.0.99 -I eth0 from a real VM, tcpdump on the tap shows the packet leaving but no reply arriving.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) IPv6 SLAAC fails to obtain the prefix, check whether ND RS is dropped at stage 2 (this requires OVN 23.06 or later). (b) A VM that performs an ARP probe (RFC 5227, source IP `0.0.0.0`) is dropped, verify that the stage allows `arp.spa=0.0.0.0` (OVN allows it). (c) Gratuitous ARP from a freshly booted VM does not update `MAC_Binding` on other chassis, check whether `port_security` already carries the IP.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `arptables -A INPUT --src-mac ... --src-ip ... -j ACCEPT` is analogous. Cisco `ip arp inspection` (DAI) is analogous and uses the DHCP snooping binding table. NSX-T DFW has dedicated rules for ARP. The OVN distinction: integrated into the declarative pipeline through the `port_security` field, no DHCP snooping required because `port_security` is filled by IPAM when the VM is created.

---

## §13.19.28 LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB, look up the MAC in the cluster-wide FDB table

**Concept.** `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` is logical stage **number 3** of LS ingress and is dedicated to **looking up the source MAC** of the packet in the `FDB` table (SBDB) to determine which port on the cluster has learned that MAC. The stage applies only to LSPs with `addresses="unknown"` or empty port_security (router peering LSPs, VLAN trunks, external gateways). When the MAC is not yet in the FDB, the downstream `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` stage learns it. Type: **pipeline FDB lookup peripheral**, subtype "MAC learning lookup gate".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced in OVN 22.03 (early 2022) when Numan Siddique (Red Hat) moved L2 learning from per-chassis OVS bridge native learning to the cluster-wide SBDB FDB table. Before OVN 22.03, each chassis learned MACs independently through the OVS bridge, which created asymmetry. The `LOOKUP_FDB` and `PUT_FDB` stages were added together with the SBDB `FDB` table (see Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.4).

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 3 in LS_IN, OF table 11 (per Part 13.16 line 154). It sits after port-security (tables 8 to 10) and before `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` (table 12). It depends on the SBDB `FDB` table populated by `controller/pinctrl.c`.

**Role.** Call the `lookup_fdb(P, A)` action to look up `(datapath, eth.src)` in the FDB. On a hit, set `REGBIT_LKUP_FDB=1` (`reg0[11]`); the next stage `PUT_FDB` then skips the insert. On a miss, `REGBIT_LKUP_FDB=0`, and `PUT_FDB` inserts a new entry.

**Why it exists.** The pre-22.03 design (OVS bridge native learning) had a problem: chassis A learned MAC X on port P; chassis B had not yet seen traffic from X, so it did not know where X lived and flooded every packet aimed at X, wasting bandwidth. A cluster-wide FDB through SBDB lets every chassis read the same data and all know where X lives.

**Problems it solves.** (a) flood storm when the cluster has just booted (nothing learned yet), (b) asymmetric MAC learning between chassis, (c) the migration learning gap when a VM moves to a new chassis.

**Importance.** Peripheral for a pure-VM LS (because LSPs have explicit `addresses`, no FDB is needed). Medium importance for an LS with many LSPs of `addresses=unknown` (router peering, VLAN trunks, external gateways).

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lswitch_lookup_fdb()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03). The function emits a flow only when the LS has at least one LSP with `addresses=unknown`. The priority 100 flow for an unknown LSP is `match=(inport == "lsp-router-peer")` with action `reg0[11] = lookup_fdb(inport, eth.src); next;`. The priority 0 default action is `next`. The `lookup_fdb` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c` and executed at runtime by `controller/pinctrl.c` function `pinctrl_handle_lookup_fdb()`.

```
table=11 (ls_in_lookup_fdb), priority=100,
  match=(inport == "lsp-router-peer"),
  action=(reg0[11] = lookup_fdb(inport, eth.src); next;)
table=11, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Understand when the stage is active: only LSes that have at least one LSP with `addresses=unknown`. Verify the FDB table with `ovn-sbctl --columns=mac,dp_key,port_key list FDB`. Debug "a freshly migrated VM floods traffic" by checking the FDB before and after migration.

**Classification.** Pipeline FDB stage. Same family as `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` (the lookup-then-learn pair). Different from `MAC_Binding` (the LR ARP cache): FDB is for L2 learning whereas `MAC_Binding` is for L3 ARP and NDP resolution.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create an LSP with `addresses=unknown` (for example, a router peering port). (2) Verify that stage 11 emits a flow for that LSP. (3) Send a packet from an unknown MAC; `ovn-trace` shows `lookup_fdb()` returning 0 and `reg0[11]=0`. (4) The `PUT_FDB` stage inserts the entry. (5) The next time the same MAC arrives, the lookup hits and `reg0[11]=1`.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: MAC traffic keeps flooding without converging. Possible causes: (a) `ovn-controller` cannot insert into the FDB due to OVSDB permissions, (b) the FDB row carries a stale `port_key` (the LSP was deleted), so the lookup hits but forwards to the wrong port, (c) `other_config:fdb_age_threshold` is too short, so entries expire before they are reused.

**Tightly related to.** The `FDB` table (SBDB), the `lookup_fdb` action, `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` (sister), `REGBIT_LKUP_FDB=reg0[11]`, and `Logical_Switch_Port.addresses=unknown`. See Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.4 for the FDB schema. Loosely coupled: `MAC_Binding` (L3 sibling) and OVS bridge native learning (an alternative path when FDB is disabled).

**Version differences.** OVN before 22.03: no FDB stage; per-chassis OVS bridge learning is used. OVN 22.03: introduces the `FDB` SBDB table and the stage pair. OVN 23.06: adds `Logical_Switch.other_config:fdb_age_threshold` (default 0 means no expiration). OVN 24.09: `ovn-controller` supports the `MAX_FDB_ENTRIES` limit (commit `fb96ae3679`, `controller/pinctrl.c`).

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl --columns=mac,dp_key,port_key list FDB
  mac      : "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:09"
  dp_key   : 5
  port_key : 8

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-with-trunk | grep ls_in_lookup_fdb
  table=3 (ls_in_lookup_fdb), priority=100,
    match=(inport == "lsp-trunk"),
    action=(reg0[11] = lookup_fdb(inport, eth.src); next;)

$ ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-trunk" && eth.src==aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:09 && ...'
  3. ls_in_lookup_fdb (priority 100): reg0[11] = lookup_fdb(...); next;
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lswitch_lookup_fdb()` (branch-22.03). Action implementation: `lib/actions.c` parses `lookup_fdb`. Runtime: `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_lookup_fdb()`. Stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` (logical table 3, OF table 11). SBDB schema: `ovn-sb.ovsschema` table `FDB`. OF table `OFTABLE_LOOKUP_FDB=72` in `controller/lflow.h` (see Part 13.16 line 64).

**Real-world incident.** Q3 2024, OVN 22.03 cluster with 200 VLAN trunk LSPs: traffic between router peerings kept flooding without converging after 30 minutes. Investigation: `ovn-sbctl list FDB | wc -l` reported 0 entries. Root cause: `ovn-controller` was in a crash loop on one chassis and could not insert into the FDB. Fix: restart the controller; the FDB populated within a few seconds. Lesson learned: monitor the FDB row count as a health check.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-trunk
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-trunk lsp-trunk-A
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-trunk-A unknown
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-trunk | grep ls_in_lookup_fdb shows the priority 100 flow for lsp-trunk-A.
# Lab task 2: send traffic from a new MAC through lsp-trunk-A; ovn-sbctl list FDB shows a new entry.
# Lab task 3: repeat the traffic; ovn-trace reports lookup_fdb hit and reg0[11]=1.
# Lab task 4: set fdb_age_threshold=10, wait 15 seconds; the entry expires and the lookup misses.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) FDB does not populate even though the unknown LSP is set up; check whether the `ovn-controller` log shows `pinctrl_handle_lookup_fdb` activity. (b) A stale FDB entry forwards to the wrong port; clean up manually with `ovn-sbctl destroy FDB <uuid>`. (c) A cluster with more than 10,000 unknown LSPs hits FDB scale issues; enable `MAX_FDB_ENTRIES` (24.09 and later) or shard the LS.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux bridge `bridge fdb show` is analogous (per-host learning). Cisco Nexus VXLAN EVPN with BGP-EVPN MAC route announcements is analogous (cluster-wide learning through a control plane). The Open vSwitch `mac-learning` module is analogous (per-bridge learning). The OVN distinction: cluster-wide through SBDB, no BGP needed, no separate control protocol needed.

---

## §13.19.29 LS_IN_PUT_FDB, learn a new MAC into the cluster-wide FDB table

**Concept.** `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` is logical stage **number 4** of LS ingress and is dedicated to **inserting or updating** an FDB entry when `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` missed (`REGBIT_LKUP_FDB=0`). The stage calls the `put_fdb(P, A)` action to push `(datapath, eth.src) -> inport` to `ovn-controller` through a controller action; `controller/pinctrl.c` then performs the SBDB `FDB` insert. Type: **pipeline FDB learning peripheral**, subtype "MAC learning insert step".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced together with `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` in OVN 22.03 (early 2022) as the learning pair. Before OVN 22.03, learning happened implicitly through OVS bridge native learning; OVN 22.03 converted it into an explicit pipeline action.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 4 in LS_IN, OF table 12 (per Part 13.16 line 155). It sits after `LOOKUP_FDB` (table 11) and before `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` (table 13).

**Role.** When `reg0[11]=0` (lookup miss), the stage calls `put_fdb(inport, eth.src)` and the `ovn-controller` upcall handler inserts a row into the SBDB FDB. When `reg0[11]=1` (already present), the stage is skipped.

**Why it exists.** Splitting lookup and learning into two stages lets the REGBIT serve as an intermediate flag, which avoids duplicate inserts and learns only when necessary. The check-then-act pattern matches `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` and `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE`.

**Problems it solves.** (a) idempotent insert (only on a miss), (b) async insert through a controller action does not block the fast path, (c) the per-chassis controller updates SBDB so other chassis read the entry immediately.

**Importance.** Same tier as `LOOKUP_FDB`. Peripheral for a pure-VM LS, medium for trunk and peering LSes.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lswitch_put_fdb()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03). The function emits flows only for LSPs with `addresses=unknown`:

```
table=12 (ls_in_put_fdb), priority=100,
  match=(reg0[11] == 0 && inport == "lsp-router-peer"),
  action=(put_fdb(inport, eth.src); next;)
table=12, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `put_fdb` action is a **controller action** (like `put_arp` and `put_dhcp_opts`): the packet is upcalled to `ovn-controller`, the handler inserts the SBDB row, and the packet is not blocked (it continues the pipeline immediately). The controller deduplicates by checking whether `(dp_key, mac)` already exists before inserting.

**How an operator masters this.** Understand that `put_fdb` is a controller action and therefore carries upcall overhead. Each new MAC causes one upcall. When the cluster has high MAC churn (for example, many ephemeral VMs), the FDB grows large and the upcall rate is high. Sizing: monitor the `ovn-controller` log and the SBDB transaction rate.

**Classification.** Pipeline FDB learning stage. Sister of `LOOKUP_FDB`. Different from `put_arp` (LR `MAC_Binding` learning): `put_fdb` is for L2 forwarding, `put_arp` is for L3 ARP resolution.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Configure the LSP with `addresses=unknown`. (2) Send traffic from a new MAC. (3) `ovn-sbctl list FDB` shows the new entry after roughly a few tens of milliseconds. (4) The next time the same MAC arrives, the lookup hits and `put` is skipped.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: the FDB does not grow even though traffic flows through. Causes: (a) `ovn-controller` upcall bottleneck (CPU, raft latency), (b) `pinctrl.c` rate limit, (c) SBDB transaction conflict that forces insert retries. Diagnose with the `ovn-controller` log and the SBDB transaction log.

**Tightly related to.** The `put_fdb` action, the `FDB` table, `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB` (sister), `REGBIT_LKUP_FDB`, and `controller/pinctrl.c` function `pinctrl_handle_put_fdb()`. See Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.4. Loosely coupled: the `put_arp` action (the LR analogue).

**Version differences.** OVN before 22.03: not present. OVN 22.03: introduced with the FDB table. OVN 23.06: adds the `fdb_age_threshold` configuration (auto-aging rows). OVN 24.09: supports the `MAX_FDB_ENTRIES` limit to prevent unbounded FDB growth (upstream commit `fb96ae3679` in `controller/pinctrl.c`, the `MAX_FDB_ENTRIES` section).

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_put_fdb
  table=4 (ls_in_put_fdb), priority=100,
    match=(reg0[11] == 0 && inport == "lsp-trunk-A"),
    action=(put_fdb(inport, eth.src); next;)

$ ovn-sbctl --columns=mac,dp_key,port_key list FDB
  mac      : "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:09"
  dp_key   : 5
  port_key : 8

$ tail -f /var/log/ovn/ovn-controller.log | grep "put_fdb"
  pinctrl|INFO|Inserting MAC aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:09 into FDB for dp 5 port 8
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lswitch_put_fdb()` (branch-22.03). Action parser: `lib/actions.c` `parse_PUT_FDB`. Runtime handler: `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_put_fdb()`. SBDB row insert through the `controller/binding.c` transaction. Stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_PUT_FDB` (logical table 4, OF table 12).

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, OVN 22.03 cluster: a VLAN trunk LSP received a burst of 5,000 new MACs per second. The result: `ovn-controller` CPU at 100 percent, SBDB raft commit lag at 10 seconds, normal packet pipeline but stale FDB, leading to wrong forwarding. Mitigation: enable `fdb_age_threshold=300`, batch upcalls in the controller (24.09 and later), and reduce churn upstream.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-learn
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-learn lsp-A
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-A unknown
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl list FDB shows 0 entries.
# Lab task 2: send 3 packets with 3 different MACs through lsp-A; ovn-sbctl list FDB shows 3 entries.
# Lab task 3: send a packet with an existing MAC; the ovn-controller log shows no new "Inserting" line (lookup hit, put skipped).
# Lab task 4: ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=12 shows n_packets increasing per unique MAC.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) FDB grows without bound (more than 100,000 entries) on a cluster with many ephemeral VMs; enable `fdb_age_threshold`. (b) `ovn-controller` restart, the FDB does not rebuild itself, and traffic floods again until the first packet triggers `put_fdb`. (c) Race condition: two chassis call `put_fdb` for the same MAC concurrently; the SBDB transaction rejects one of them and logs a warning.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux bridge `bridge fdb add ... dynamic` is analogous (kernel learning). Cisco VLAN MAC learning is analogous (hardware CAM). VMware VDS MAC learning is analogous (per-host). The OVN distinction: an explicit pipeline action plus cluster-wide propagation through SBDB; the control plane can observe what was learned and can intervene through a manual `ovn-sbctl create FDB`.

---

## §13.19.30 LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL, prepare the conntrack zone before ACL or LB evaluation

**Concept.** `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` is logical stage **number 7** of LS ingress, sitting between the pre-stage pair (`LS_IN_PRE_ACL` stage 5 and `LS_IN_PRE_LB` stage 6) and the real evaluation stages (`LS_IN_ACL_HINT` stage 8, `LS_IN_ACL` stage 9, `LS_IN_LB` stage 12). The stage emits `ct_next` to load `ct_state` (the conntrack state register) into the pipeline when `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_DEFRAG` or `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT` was set by `PRE_ACL` or `PRE_LB`. Type: **pipeline stateful preparer peripheral**, subtype "conntrack context loader for ingress".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced in OVN 2.7 (2017) when Numan Siddique implemented full stateful ACL plus LB. Before OVN 2.7, conntrack only existed for stateless ACL; adding stateful LB required a pre-stage to prepare the zone register (`reg13`) and emit `ct_next` at the right moment so that downstream stages could read `ct.new`, `ct.est`, `ct.rel`, and `ct.inv`.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 7 in LS_IN, OF table 15 (per Part 13.16 line 158). It sits after `LS_IN_PRE_LB` (table 14) and before `LS_IN_ACL_HINT` (table 16).

**Role.** (a) read `reg13` (the conntrack zone of the LSP, already set in `PORT_SEC_IP` stage 1), (b) emit `ct_next(zone=reg13)` so the datapath performs the conntrack lookup, then `ct_state` becomes available in the downstream stages, (c) skip when neither `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_DEFRAG` nor `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT` is set (the LS has no stateful ACL or LB).

**Why it exists.** Separate "set zone and prepare" from the evaluation stages. Reason: `ACL_HINT`, `ACL`, and `LB` need `ct_state` resolved already; if `ct_next` were emitted in the evaluation stage, the action and the emit would happen together and the flow rules would be complex and hard to debug. Splitting stages keeps single-responsibility.

**Problems it solves.** (a) conntrack zone setup at the right moment (after the LSP is known, before evaluation), (b) avoid duplicate `ct_next` calls when stateful ACL and LB are both active, (c) let an LS without stateful needs skip the entire conntrack overhead.

**Importance.** Peripheral in complexity but critical for every LS with stateful ACL or LB. If this stage misbehaves, both ACL and LB are affected.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_pre_stateful()` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03). The function emits flows along two branches: emit `ct_next` when the defrag or nat REGBIT is set, and skip otherwise. Example:

```
table=15 (ls_in_pre_stateful), priority=120,
  match=(reg0[2] == 1 || reg0[10] == 1),  // REGBIT_CONNTRACK_DEFRAG hoặc REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT
  action=(ct_next;)
table=15, priority=110,
  match=(reg0[6] == 1),  // REGBIT_LOAD_BALANCER
  action=(ct_lb_mark;)
table=15, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

When `ct_next` runs, the datapath looks up the conntrack table with the five-tuple plus the zone (from `reg13`) and sets `ct.new=1` for a new connection, `ct.est=1` for an established connection, and so on. The downstream stage (`ACL_HINT`) reads these bits to decide the verdict.

**How an operator masters this.** Understand that `reg13` holds the conntrack zone of the LSP (set in `PORT_SEC_IP`). Understand the chain: `PRE_ACL` and `PRE_LB` set REGBITs, `PRE_STATEFUL` emits `ct_next`, then `ACL` and `LB` read the `ct.*` flags. When stateful ACL misbehaves (for example, `allow-related` does not work), check these four stages in sequence with `ovn-trace`.

**Classification.** Pipeline stateful preparer. Symmetric counterpart of `LS_OUT_PRE_STATEFUL` (egress, OF table 42, see Part 13.16 line 195). Same family as `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` and `LS_IN_PRE_LB` (which set REGBITs), and `LS_IN_ACL_HINT` and `LS_IN_LB` (which consume `ct_state`).

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create an LS with stateful ACL and LB. (2) Verify that stage 15 emits priority 120 (defrag) and 110 (LB) flows. (3) Send a new connection; `ovn-trace` reports `ct.new=1` after stage 15. (4) Send a reply packet; `ct.est=1`.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: an `allow-related` ACL does not let reply traffic pass. Possible causes: (a) `PRE_STATEFUL` does not emit `ct_next` because the REGBIT is not set, (b) `reg13 = 0` (the zone is not set in `PORT_SEC_IP` because the LSP has no port_security), (c) conntrack zone collision with the egress stage.

**Tightly related to.** `reg13` (the conntrack zone), `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_DEFRAG=reg0[2]`, `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT=reg0[10]`, `REGBIT_LOAD_BALANCER=reg0[6]`, the `ct_next` and `ct_lb_mark` actions, `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` and `LS_IN_PRE_LB` (sisters that set the REGBITs), and `LS_IN_ACL_HINT`, `LS_IN_ACL`, and `LS_IN_LB` (consumers). See Part 13.18 for the REGBIT catalogue. Loosely coupled: `LS_OUT_PRE_STATEFUL` (the egress analogue).

**Version differences.** OVN 2.7 (2017): introduced the `PRE`/`STATEFUL` pair. OVN 21.06: added the `ct_lb_mark` action to replace the old `ct_lb`. OVN 22.03: stable; `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT` introduced for the LR NAT case. OVN 24.03: compile-time optimisation when an LS has more than 100 stateful ACLs.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_pre_stateful
  table=7 (ls_in_pre_stateful), priority=120,
    match=(reg0[2] == 1 || reg0[10] == 1), action=(ct_next;)
  table=7, priority=110, match=(reg0[6] == 1), action=(ct_lb_mark;)
  table=7, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-vm1" && eth.src==aa:... && ip4.src==10.0.0.5 && ip4.dst==10.0.0.6 && tcp && tcp.dst==80'
   7. ls_in_pre_stateful (priority 120): ct_next;
       ct_state: new
   ...

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=15 | head -3
  cookie=0x..., table=15, priority=120, reg0=0x4/0x4, actions=ct(table=16,zone=NXM_NX_REG13[0..15])
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_pre_stateful()` (branch-22.03). Action parser: `lib/actions.c` `parse_CT_NEXT`, `parse_CT_LB`. Stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` (logical table 7, OF table 15). The conntrack zone semantics are documented in `ovn-northd.8.xml` under "Ingress Table 7".

**Real-world incident.** Q2 2025, OVN 22.03: `allow-related` ACL for HTTP and HTTPS, but the VM did not receive the reply traffic. `ovn-trace` for the reply packet showed stage 7 emitting `ct_next` but `ct_state=inv` instead of `est`, so ACL dropped. Root cause: the VM used IPv6 but port_security did not include IPv6, so `reg13` was not set in stage 1 and `ct_zone=0` collided with the egress zone. Fix: added IPv6 to port_security; `reg13` was set correctly, `ct_state=est`, and the reply was allowed through.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-stateful
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-stateful lsp-vm1
sudo ovn-nbctl acl-add ls-stateful from-lport 1000 'inport == "lsp-vm1" && tcp.dst == 80' allow-related
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-stateful | grep ls_in_pre_stateful shows the priority 120 flow active.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace a TCP SYN from lsp-vm1; stage 7 emits ct_next and reports ct.new=1.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace the TCP SYN-ACK reply; stage 7 emits ct_next, ct.est=1, and ACL allows it.
# Lab task 4: temporarily remove the stateful ACL; the priority 120 flow disappears and stage 7 carries only priority 0 with next.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) `ct_state=inv` instead of `est` on the reply, the `reg13` zone has collided or port_security is missing IPv4 or IPv6. (b) Stage 7 does not emit `ct_next` even though stateful ACL is present, check whether `PRE_ACL` set `reg0[2]`. (c) Stateful LB works but `allow-related` ACL does not, check the `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT` versus `DEFRAG` separation.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux netfilter `iptables -t raw -A PREROUTING -j CT --zone N` is analogous (zone setup before conntrack lookup). NSX-T DFW connection tracking is analogous (per-segment zone). HAProxy stateful proxy has a similar "frontend prepare" concept before backend matching. The OVN distinction: the declarative pipeline uses REGBITs as flags between stages, which lets the entire conntrack overhead be skipped when the LS does not need stateful processing.

---

## §13.19.31 LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR, look up the next-hop MAC_Binding on LR ingress

**Concept.** `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` is logical stage **number 1** of LR ingress, immediately after `LR_IN_ADMISSION` (stage 0). The stage looks up the `MAC_Binding` cache (IP to MAC plus outport) for `xxreg0` (the next-hop IP carried inside the pipeline) or `eth.dst` (for packets that already carry a destination MAC). On a hit, set `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]=1`; on a miss, leave that bit at 0 and let `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` handle it. Type: **pipeline ingress neighbor cache lookup peripheral**, subtype "MAC_Binding cache reader".

**History and background.** The lookup/learn neighbor pair was introduced in OVN 21.03 (early 2021) when Numan Siddique split ARP and ND resolution into two explicit stages to avoid duplicate ARP requests and to allow stateful policy between lookup and learn. Before OVN 21.03, the MAC_Binding lookup was embedded in `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE`; splitting the stage lets a REGBIT serve as the intermediate flag. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline) is stable.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 1 in LR_IN, mapped to OF table 9 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 217). It sits after `LR_IN_ADMISSION` (table 8) and before `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (table 10). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath. Only packets that already passed admission (with `eth.dst` matching the LRP MAC) reach this stage.

**Role.** A pre-flight check on the neighbor cache: on a hit, the packet continues normal routing without emitting an ARP or ND request; on a miss, the learn stage emits an ARP or ND request. Set `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` as the flag that informs the downstream stage of the decision.

**Why it exists.** In a distributed router, every chassis needs to know the next-hop MAC before rewriting `eth.dst`. Emitting an ARP request for every packet is too expensive, so a cache is needed. Splitting lookup and learn keeps the operation idempotent and avoids an ARP storm when many packets target a next-hop with no known MAC yet.

**Problems it solves.** (a) avoid an ARP or ND request per packet (fast path on a cache hit), (b) reduce broadcast on the overlay, (c) allow `MAC_Binding` to be pre-populated through `Static_MAC_Binding` (config-driven), (d) create a policy injection point between lookup and learn (absent before OVN 21.03).

**Importance.** Peripheral in complexity but foundational for every LR with real routing. If this stage misbehaves, every packet exiting the LR has to wait for ARP resolution; latency spikes and the overlay floods with ARP traffic.

**How it works.** The flow generation function follows the upstream OVN naming convention: `build_lrouter_flows_lookup_neighbor()` or equivalent in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; the stage token is `S_ROUTER_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`). The function emits a priority 100 flow with match `xxreg0 == <known IP>` and action `eth.dst = <cached MAC>; reg9[2] = 1; next;`, plus a priority 0 catch-all with action `next;`. Data source: the SBDB `MAC_Binding` table that `ovn-controller` compiles per-chassis into lflow. Example flow on LR `lr-main` that already has `MAC_Binding (10.0.0.10, aa:bb:cc:00:00:10)`:

```
table=9 (lr_in_lookup_neighbor), priority=100,
  match=(xxreg0 == 10.0.0.10),
  action=(eth.dst = aa:bb:cc:00:00:10; reg9[2] = 1; next;)
table=9, priority=0, match=(1), action=(reg9[2] = 0; next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Read `MAC_Binding` with `ovn-sbctl list MAC_Binding`; dump the stage flows with `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_lookup_neighbor`; trace the hit and miss context with `ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp1" && ip4.dst == 10.0.0.10'` and check whether `reg9[2]` is set to 1. When the cluster has high next-hop churn, monitor the `MAC_Binding` row count and check whether the `ovn-controller` log carries the warning "deleting stale MAC_Binding".

**Classification.** Pipeline ingress neighbor cache stage. Sister of `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (stage 2). Different from `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB`: `MAC_Binding` is for L3 (IP to MAC) on LR, FDB is for L2 (MAC to port) on LS.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create the LR, LRP, and route. (2) Send the first packet to a next-hop with no known MAC; this stage misses, the LEARN stage takes over. (3) After the ARP reply, `MAC_Binding` is inserted into SBDB. (4) The second packet to the same next-hop hits the fast path through this stage. (5) Optional: pre-populate through `Static_MAC_Binding` to skip the first ARP round.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: traffic through the LR has about 1 second of delay on the first packet and then runs cleanly. Common cause: `MAC_Binding` is not yet cached, so ARP must complete first. If the delay persists: (a) SBDB `MAC_Binding` is repeatedly cleared because the aging timer is too low (run `ovn-nbctl set NB_Global . options:mac_binding_age_threshold=0` to disable aging), (b) the static route points to a next-hop outside the LRP subnet, so ARP keeps failing, (c) `ovn-controller` has not synced `MAC_Binding` after a crash.

**Tightly related to.** The SBDB `MAC_Binding` table (columns `ip`, `mac`, `logical_port`, `datapath`), the NBDB `Static_MAC_Binding` table (config-driven entries), `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]` (verify Part 13.18 register catalogue), `xxreg0` (next-hop IP register after routing), `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (sister stage 2), and `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` (downstream consumer; it still exists in the pipeline but now plays a slimmer role since the lookup and learn split). Loosely coupled: the Linux kernel `neigh` table (an alternative stack outside OVN) and VRRP MAC binding (router redundancy).

**Version differences.** OVN before 21.03: no dedicated stage (the logic was inside `ARP_RESOLVE`). OVN 21.03: split lookup and learn. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable; the NBDB `Static_MAC_Binding` table was added. OVN 23.06: added the `mac_binding_age_threshold` option to allow per-LR auto-aging. OVN 24.03: no semantic change.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl --columns=ip,mac,logical_port list MAC_Binding
  ip            : "10.0.0.10"
  mac           : "aa:bb:cc:00:00:10"
  logical_port  : "lrp1"

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_lookup_neighbor
  table=1 (lr_in_lookup_neighbor), priority=100,
    match=(xxreg0 == 10.0.0.10),
    action=(eth.dst = aa:bb:cc:00:00:10; reg9[2] = 1; next;)
  table=1, priority=0, match=(1), action=(reg9[2] = 0; next;)

$ ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp-uplink" && eth.dst == <lrp-mac> && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 && ip4.dst == 10.0.0.10'
   1. lr_in_lookup_neighbor (priority 100): eth.dst = aa:bb:cc:00:00:10; reg9[2] = 1; next;
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` flow generation functions for stage `S_ROUTER_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (verified that the token exists in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). The `get_arp` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`. Stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (logical table 1, OF table 9). SBDB schema: `ovn-sb.ovsschema` table `MAC_Binding`. Register `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]` in `include/ovn/logical-fields.h`. See Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.3 for the `MAC_Binding` deep dive.

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, OpenStack-on-OVN cluster with 50 tenant LRs and gateway redundancy through `HA_Chassis_Group`: north-south traffic sometimes saw 1 to 2 seconds of delay after gateway failover. `ovn-trace` reported that the first packet after failover missed at table 9 `lr_in_lookup_neighbor` (the `MAC_Binding` was stale, inherited from the old chassis). Root cause: the newly active `ovn-controller` had not yet synced fresh `MAC_Binding` rows from SBDB. Mitigation: increase the controller probe interval and pre-populate `Static_MAC_Binding` for the external gateway.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-test
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-test lrp-test 00:00:00:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-test 0.0.0.0/0 10.0.0.254
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl list MAC_Binding shows 0 entries initially.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace lr-test with ip4.dst=10.0.0.10 reports lr_in_lookup_neighbor priority 0 (miss), reg9[2]=0.
# Lab task 3: ovn-nbctl create Static_MAC_Binding ip=10.0.0.10 mac=...; the lookup now hits and reg9[2]=1.
# Lab task 4: ovn-sbctl list MAC_Binding | wc -l tracks the growth of the row count.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) `MAC_Binding` keeps missing for the same IP, the next-hop is outside the subnet or unreachable. (b) `MAC_Binding` is stale after a gateway failover, the controller has not synced. (c) Cache poisoning (the wrong `MAC_Binding` for the gateway IP), clean up manually with `ovn-sbctl destroy MAC_Binding <uuid>`.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux kernel `neigh` cache (`ip neigh show`) is analogous per host. Cisco IOS ARP cache (`show ip arp`) is analogous per router. VMware NSX-T MAC binding through central control is analogous, cluster-wide. The OVN distinction: cluster-wide through SBDB so every chassis shares the cache; pre-population through `Static_MAC_Binding`; the lookup/learn split allows policy injection.

---

## §13.19.32 LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR, learn a new MAC_Binding through ARP and ND requests

**Concept.** `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` is logical stage **number 2** of LR ingress, immediately after `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`. When `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]==0` (lookup miss), this stage calls `put_arp(P, IP, MAC)` for IPv4 or `put_nd(P, IP, MAC)` for IPv6, pushing a new entry into the SBDB `MAC_Binding` table through a controller upcall, and at the same time emits an ARP or ND request out the outport to learn the MAC from the next-hop. Type: **pipeline ingress neighbor learning peripheral**, subtype "MAC_Binding insert step".

**History and background.** Split out in the same OVN 21.03 wave as `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`. Before OVN 21.03, the learning logic was embedded in `ARP_RESOLVE` with no intermediate flag between lookup and emitting the ARP request. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline) is stable; OVN 23.06 added rate-limiting for `put_arp`.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 2 in LR_IN, OF table 10 (per Part 13.16 line 218). It sits after `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (table 9) and before `LR_IN_IP_INPUT` (table 11). Scope: per-packet; only packets with `reg9[2]==0` (lookup miss) enter the learn branch.

**Role.** (a) when a packet arrives for a next-hop with no known MAC, call `put_arp` or `put_nd` to learn from a future ARP or ND reply; (b) emit an ARP request packet out the outport (a controller action); (c) let the current packet continue the pipeline (no drop; the packet may be retransmitted later once the MAC is learned).

**Why it exists.** The pipeline needs a single place to handle "neighbor unknown" without blocking the fast path. Splitting learn from lookup gives: learning only on a miss (idempotent), no duplicate learning logic, and a policy injection point in between.

**Problems it solves.** (a) insert `MAC_Binding` when the next-hop replies to ARP, (b) avoid duplicate ARP requests for the same IP within the rate-limit window, (c) async upcall does not block the packet flow, (d) the per-chassis controller updates SBDB so the entry is visible cluster-wide.

**Importance.** Same tier as `LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`. Critical for dynamic next-hop scenarios (a DHCP-leased gateway, a mobile VM). A static gateway can skip this through `Static_MAC_Binding`.

**How it works.** The flow generation function follows the OVN naming convention: `build_lrouter_flows_*` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (the token is verified in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). The function emits two flow groups: (a) priority 100 with match `arp || nd_ns || nd_na` (an arriving ARP or ND packet) and action `put_arp(inport, arp.spa, arp.sha); next;`; (b) priority 90 with match `reg9[2] == 0 && (ip4 || ip6)` and action `lookup_arp(inport, xxreg0, eth.dst); next;` (a controller lookup that emits an ARP request if needed). Example:

```
table=10 (lr_in_learn_neighbor), priority=100,
  match=(arp.op == 1 || arp.op == 2),
  action=(put_arp(inport, arp.spa, arp.sha); next;)
table=10, priority=100,
  match=(nd_ns || nd_na),
  action=(put_nd(inport, nd.target, nd.tll); next;)
table=10, priority=90,
  match=(reg9[2] == 0 && (ip4 || ip6)),
  action=(lookup_arp(inport, xxreg0, eth.dst); next;)
table=10, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `put_arp` action is a **controller action** like `put_fdb` and `put_dhcp_opts`: the packet is upcalled to `ovn-controller`, the handler `pinctrl_handle_put_arp_or_nd()` inserts the SBDB row, and the packet is not blocked.

**How an operator masters this.** Understand that `put_arp` is a controller action, so there is an upcall cost for each new MAC. A cluster with high next-hop churn (for example, mobile VMs or ephemeral gateways) has a high rate, which makes `ovn-controller` CPU spike. Sizing: monitor the SBDB transaction rate and the `MAC_Binding` table size; consider enabling `mac_binding_age_threshold` to age stale rows.

**Classification.** Pipeline ingress neighbor learning stage. Sister of `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (stage 1). Different from `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` (L2 learning on LS): `LEARN_NEIGHBOR` learns IP to MAC through ARP and ND, whereas `PUT_FDB` learns MAC to port through the data plane.

**Usage workflow.** (1) The cluster has an LR with routes. (2) The first packet arrives for an unknown next-hop; stage 1 misses and stage 2 emits the ARP request. (3) The next-hop replies; the ARP packet returns through stage 2 and `put_arp` inserts a `MAC_Binding`. (4) The second packet to the same next-hop hits the fast path at stage 1.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: `MAC_Binding` does not grow even though traffic flows through the LR. Causes: (a) `ovn-controller` upcall bottleneck, (b) `pinctrl.c` rate limit blocks `put_arp`, (c) the ARP reply does not return (the next-hop is unreachable, or a firewall upstream blocks it), (d) the outport is wrong when the LR has multiple LRPs on the same subnet. Diagnose: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=10` to inspect `n_packets`, check whether the `ovn-controller` log shows `put_arp` lines, and `tcpdump` ARP on the fabric.

**Tightly related to.** The `put_arp`, `put_nd`, and `lookup_arp` actions in `lib/actions.c`, the SBDB `MAC_Binding` table, `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (sister stage 1), `controller/pinctrl.c` function `pinctrl_handle_put_arp_or_nd()`, and `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]`. See Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.3 for the `MAC_Binding` sister and Part 13.18 for the register catalogue. Loosely coupled: `LS_IN_PUT_FDB` (the L2 learning analogue).

**Version differences.** OVN before 21.03: this logic lived in `ARP_RESOLVE`. OVN 21.03: split into a separate stage. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: added rate-limit for `put_arp` to avoid storms when many misses happen at once. OVN 24.03: improved deduplication when many packets wait for the same MAC.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_learn_neighbor
  table=2 (lr_in_learn_neighbor), priority=100,
    match=(arp), action=(put_arp(inport, arp.spa, arp.sha); next;)
  table=2, priority=90,
    match=(reg9[2] == 0 && ip4),
    action=(lookup_arp(inport, xxreg0, eth.dst); next;)

$ tail -f /var/log/ovn/ovn-controller.log | grep -E "put_arp|put_nd"
  pinctrl|INFO|Inserting MAC_Binding ip=10.0.0.10 mac=aa:bb:cc:00:00:10 logical_port=lrp1

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=10 | head -3
  cookie=0x..., table=10, priority=100, arp, actions=controller(...)
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (the token is verified in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). Action parser: `lib/actions.c` `parse_PUT_ARP`, `parse_LOOKUP_ARP`. Runtime handler: `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_put_arp_or_nd()`. SBDB row insert through the `controller/binding.c` transaction. Stage token: logical table 2, OF table 10.

**Real-world incident.** Q3 2024, OVN 22.03 cluster: a tenant LR had more than 100 static routes to external next-hops on VLAN 100; the first packet to each next-hop triggered an ARP storm right after a chassis restart (every flow missed at once). Mitigation: pre-populate `Static_MAC_Binding` for the 20 most important next-hops; enable a `put_arp` rate-limit of 50 entries per second; effect: the ARP storm dropped by 90 percent.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-learn
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-learn lrp-up 00:00:00:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-learn 192.168.1.0/24 10.0.0.254
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl list MAC_Binding | grep 10.0.0.254 shows 0 entries.
# Lab task 2: send a packet with ip4.dst=192.168.1.5 through lr-learn; the ovn-controller log shows "put_arp 10.0.0.254".
# Lab task 3: ovn-sbctl list MAC_Binding | grep 10.0.0.254 shows one new entry after a few tens of milliseconds.
# Lab task 4: send a second packet; the ovn-controller log shows no new put_arp line (lookup hit, fast path).
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) `MAC_Binding` does not grow even with traffic flowing, the `ovn-controller` upcall failed, check the log and the raft latency. (b) ARP storm at cluster restart, pre-populate `Static_MAC_Binding`. (c) `MAC_Binding` learned incorrectly (the IP of one VM mapped to the wrong MAC), check spoofing policy and destroy the row manually.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux kernel `neigh` learning through arptables is analogous (per host). Cisco IOS dynamic ARP entries learned on reply are analogous (per router). Open vSwitch native ARP learning in bridge mode is analogous (per bridge). The OVN distinction: an explicit pipeline action plus cluster-wide propagation through SBDB; the learn step is separate and policy can be injected between lookup and learn.

---

## §13.19.33 LR_IN_IP_ROUTING, the main IP routing table of the LR

**Concept.** `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` is logical stage **number 11** of LR ingress and is the **main IP routing table** of the `Logical_Router`. The stage matches `ip4.dst` or `ip6.dst` against the prefix set: (a) connected subnets from `Logical_Router_Port.networks` and (b) static routes from `Logical_Router_Static_Route` (NBDB). On the longest-prefix match, it sets `xxreg0 = <next-hop IP>`, `eth.src = <LRP_MAC of the outport>`, `outport = <LRP egress>`, decrements the TTL, and then runs `next;`. Type: **pipeline core L3 forwarding decision peripheral**, classified peripheral by grouping but functionally central, subtype "FIB longest-prefix-match".

**History and background.** The stage has existed since the first OVN release (2015) as core LR functionality. The token name changed slightly across versions: `S_ROUTER_IN_IP_ROUTING` has been stable since OVN 2.6. OVN 21.06 added `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_PRE` (stage 10) to pre-process policy hints before entering the main routing stage. OVN 22.06 added ECMP hash registers for the `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` follow-up.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 11 in LR_IN, OF table 19 (per Part 13.16 line 227). It sits after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_PRE` (table 18) and before `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` (table 20). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath. Every packet not handled by an earlier stage (admission, IP_INPUT, DNAT) reaches this stage so the outport can be decided.

**Role.** (a) longest-prefix match the destination IP against the LR's whole FIB, (b) set `xxreg0` as the next-hop IP for the neighbor lookup follow-up, (c) set `outport` to the egress LRP, (d) decrement the TTL (action `dec_ttl`), (e) when no entry matches, set `outport = drop` or fall through so a downstream stage can handle the unreachable case.

**Why it exists.** Every LR needs a declarative FIB that `ovn-northd` compiles from NBDB into lflow. This stage is the prefix-to-next-hop match point, the centre of routing functionality. Without this stage, the LR would only do L2 transit with no routing.

**Problems it solves.** (a) forwarding decision based on the destination IP, (b) support both connected and static routes, (c) form the foundation for ECMP (many equal-cost routes with the same prefix; the ECMP stage picks one), (d) form the foundation for policy-based routing (the POLICY stage can override the decision).

**Importance.** Peripheral when counted by complexity grouping, but functionally **very important** (every LR uses it). The peripheral label reflects sizing only, not the real value tier.

**How it works.** Flow generation function follows the OVN naming convention: `build_lrouter_ip_routing_flows()` or equivalent in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_IP_ROUTING`). The function iterates over each `Logical_Router_Static_Route` and each `Logical_Router_Port.networks` entry of the LR and emits a flow whose priority depends on the prefix length (longer prefix wins, longest-prefix match achieved naturally through OpenFlow priority). The action sets `xxreg0 = <next-hop IP>`, `reg0 = <output reg>`, `eth.src = <LRP_MAC>`, `outport = <LRP egress>`, `flags.loopback = 0`, then `next;`. Example on LR `lr-main` with the connected `10.0.0.0/24` through `lrp1` and a static default route to `10.0.0.254`:

```
table=19 (lr_in_ip_routing), priority=49,
  match=(reg7 == 0 && ip4.dst == 10.0.0.0/24),
  action=(ip.ttl--; reg8[0..15] = 0; xxreg0 = ip4.dst; eth.src = 00:00:00:00:00:01;
          outport = "lrp1"; flags.loopback = 1; next;)
table=19, priority=1,
  match=(reg7 == 0 && ip4.dst == 0.0.0.0/0),
  action=(ip.ttl--; reg8[0..15] = 0; xxreg0 = 10.0.0.254; eth.src = 00:00:00:00:00:01;
          outport = "lrp1"; flags.loopback = 0; next;)
table=19, priority=0, match=(1), action=(drop;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Read the LR FIB with `ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr-main`; dump the stage flows with `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_ip_routing`; trace the routing decision with `ovn-trace lr-main 'ip4.dst == 10.0.0.10'` to see which priority matches; debug "no route to host" by checking that `ovn-trace` reports stage 19 priority 0 drop. Understand the priority encoding: prefix length 24 maps to priority around 49, length 0 maps to priority 1, and the default drop is priority 0.

**Classification.** Pipeline core L3 forwarding decision. Sister of `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` (stage 12, ECMP hash) and `LR_IN_POLICY` (stage 13, policy override). Different from `LS_IN_L2_LKUP`: this stage does L3 longest-prefix matching versus the L2 exact match on the destination MAC.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create the LR and an LRP with `networks="10.0.0.1/24"`; the connected route is auto-generated. (2) `ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 10.0.0.254` adds the static route. (3) Verify the FIB with `lr-route-list`. (4) Send a packet; `ovn-trace` reports which priority matches. (5) Tunable: the priority can be overridden through the `--policy=<src-ip>` flag to inject source-routing.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: traffic to the destination keeps dropping. Diagnose: `ovn-trace lr-main 'ip4.dst == <ip>'` to see which priority stage 19 matches. If priority 0 (the default drop), a route is missing. If a high priority matches but the outport is wrong, the LRP is misconfigured. If `TTL=1` after the decrement, the packet entered stage 19 but `IP_INPUT` already dropped it earlier.

**Tightly related to.** The `Logical_Router_Static_Route` table in NBDB (columns `ip_prefix`, `nexthop`, `output_port`, `policy`, `route_table`), `Logical_Router_Port.networks` (connected route source), `xxreg0` (next-hop IP register), `outport` (egress LRP), the `dec_ttl` and `ip.ttl--` actions, `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_PRE` (the immediately preceding stage that sets the policy hint), `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` (the immediately following stage that hashes to pick an ECMP path), and `LR_IN_POLICY` (a stage that can override the decision). See Part 13.3 §LR_Static_Route. Loosely coupled: the BGP FIB (out of scope per the North Star) and Linux `ip route` (an alternative stack).

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6 onwards: the stage is stable. OVN 21.06: added `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_PRE` for pre-processing. OVN 22.06: added ECMP hash registers and the `route_table` column in NBDB to support multi-VRF logic per LR. OVN 24.03: compile-time optimisation when an LR has more than 10,000 routes (FIB compression).

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr-main
  IPv4 Routes
  Route Table <main>:
              10.0.0.0/24                  dst-ip
              0.0.0.0/0       10.0.0.254   dst-ip lrp1

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_ip_routing
  table=11 (lr_in_ip_routing), priority=49,
    match=(reg7 == 0 && ip4.dst == 10.0.0.0/24), action=(...)
  table=11, priority=1,
    match=(reg7 == 0 && ip4.dst == 0.0.0.0/0), action=(... xxreg0 = 10.0.0.254; ...)

$ ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp-uplink" && ... && ip4.dst == 1.2.3.4'
  11. lr_in_ip_routing (priority 1): xxreg0 = 10.0.0.254; outport = "lrp1"; next;
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_IP_ROUTING` (verified token in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). Stage token: logical table 11, OF table 19. NBDB schema: `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `Logical_Router_Static_Route`. The `dec_ttl` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`. Documented in `ovn-northd.8.xml` under "Ingress Table 11: IP Routing".

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, OpenStack cluster with 200 tenant LRs: east-west traffic between two subnets within the same tenant occasionally dropped. `ovn-trace` reported that stage 19 matched priority 1 (the default route) instead of priority 49 (the connected route). Root cause: a Neutron driver bug emitted the `0.0.0.0/0` static route with a priority that overrode the connected route. Fix: downstream Neutron driver patch keeps the connected route at a higher priority than the default.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-fib
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-fib lrp-int 00:00:00:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-fib lrp-ext 00:00:00:00:00:02 192.168.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-fib 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.0.254
# Lab task 1: ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr-fib shows 1 default plus 2 connected.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace lr-fib with ip4.dst=10.0.0.5 hits priority 49 connected match.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace lr-fib with ip4.dst=8.8.8.8 hits priority 1 default match.
# Lab task 4: lr-route-add 8.8.8.0/24 192.168.0.253; ovn-trace ip4.dst=8.8.8.8 now hits priority 25 new route.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) traffic drop at stage 19 priority 0, a route is missing. (b) asymmetric routing (the forward path goes through LR1, the return path through LR2), check the ECMP hash or check the route prefix on each side. (c) routing loop (TTL exceeded at stage `IP_INPUT`), check for circular routes between LRs.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux kernel FIB (`ip route`) longest-prefix match is analogous. Cisco IOS routing table CEF is analogous. VMware NSX-T tier-1 routing is analogous. The OVN distinction: declarative through the NBDB `Logical_Router_Static_Route` table, `ovn-northd` compiles it into per-LR lflow, and `ovn-controller` per chassis installs OF rules from the lflow; the entire FIB is visible cluster-wide through SBDB.

---

## §13.19.34 LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP, choose the ECMP next-hop through a five-tuple hash

**Concept.** `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` is logical stage **number 12** of LR ingress, immediately after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING`. When a prefix has multiple equal-cost next-hops (multiple `Logical_Router_Static_Route` rows with the same `ip_prefix` and metric), the previous stage sets the ECMP flag and lets this stage pick one next-hop through a five-tuple hash on `ip.src`, `ip.dst`, `proto`, `tp_src`, and `tp_dst`. The `select(reg8, member1, member2, ...)` action picks a member based on the hash. Type: **pipeline ECMP load-balance peripheral**, subtype "five-tuple consistent hash next-hop selector".

**History and background.** The ECMP stage was introduced in OVN 22.06 (mid-2022) as a first-class feature. Before OVN 22.06, ECMP could be achieved through manual flow hacks but it was not native. OVN 22.06 added the `ECMP` group and the `--ecmp` flag for `lr-route-add`. OVN 23.06 added symmetric reply (see `LR_IN_ECMP_STATEFUL` stage 7).

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 12 in LR_IN, OF table 20 (per Part 13.16 line 228). It sits after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (table 19) and before `LR_IN_POLICY` (table 21). Scope: per-packet; only packets marked as ECMP candidates by stage 11 enter the select branch.

**Role.** (a) when a prefix has at least two equal-cost next-hops, hash the five-tuple and pick one next-hop deterministically, (b) ensure that packets in the same flow (same five-tuple) always pick the same path, avoiding out-of-order delivery, (c) load-balance traffic across N gateways or uplinks, (d) form the foundation for `LR_IN_ECMP_STATEFUL` (stage 7) when symmetric reply is needed.

**Why it exists.** Data centre scale-out needs to load-balance traffic across multiple gateways or uplinks. Before native ECMP, OVN used workarounds such as static-route metric tweaks or external ECMP on the fabric. The ECMP stage makes ECMP declarative in NBDB.

**Problems it solves.** (a) load-balance north-south traffic across multiple gateways, (b) five-tuple hash provides per-flow consistency (does not break TCP), (c) failure handling: when a gateway goes down, the ECMP group excludes that member and traffic redistributes, (d) symmetric reply (stage 7 stateful) sends return traffic back through the same gateway as the forward path.

**Importance.** Peripheral when grouped by usage. Critical for clusters with two or more gateway uplinks. A single-gateway lab does not use it.

**How it works.** The flow generation function follows the OVN naming convention: `build_lrouter_ecmp_flows()` or equivalent in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP`). The function iterates over each ECMP route group (two or more routes with the same prefix and metric) and emits a high-priority flow with match `reg8[0..15] == <ecmp_group_id>` and action `select(reg8 = HASH(ip.src, ip.dst, ip.proto, tp.src, tp.dst); xxreg0 = ...; outport = ...; next;)`. The number of `select` members equals the number of next-hops in the group. Example with two members `192.168.1.254` and `192.168.2.254`:

```
table=20 (lr_in_ip_routing_ecmp), priority=200,
  match=(reg8[0..15] == 1 && ip.ttl > 1),
  action=(reg8[16..31] = select(1, 2);
          xxreg0 = (reg8[16..31] == 1 ? 192.168.1.254 : 192.168.2.254);
          outport = (reg8[16..31] == 1 ? "lrp-up1" : "lrp-up2");
          eth.src = ...;
          next;)
table=20, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Understand that the five-tuple hash applies only when the packet carries L4 (TCP or UDP); ICMP uses a different hash (three-tuple). Understand that when a gateway goes down, `ovn-northd` must recompile the ECMP group, which takes a few tens of milliseconds. Use `ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.254 --ecmp` plus a second identical command with a different next-hop to create the group. Verify with `lr-route-list` that two routes share the prefix.

**Classification.** Pipeline ECMP selector. Sister of `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (stage 11, FIB lookup) and `LR_IN_ECMP_STATEFUL` (stage 7, symmetric reply). Different from `LS_IN_LB` (LB DNAT): ECMP balances gateway and uplink traffic; LB balances service backends.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create the LR plus two uplink LRPs. (2) `ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.254 --ecmp` and `lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.2.254 --ecmp`. (3) Verify the ECMP group through `lr-route-list`. (4) Send 100 flows with different five-tuples; the traffic spreads roughly 50/50 between the two next-hops. (5) Tear down one next-hop; 100 percent of traffic switches to the remaining next-hop after `ovn-northd` recompiles.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: ECMP does not load-balance and 100 percent of traffic uses one next-hop. Diagnose: (a) check `lr-route-list` to confirm two routes share the prefix and both carry `--ecmp`, (b) check that the five-tuple is varied enough (with only one flow the hash is deterministic and picks only one path), (c) check that `ovn-northd` compiled the ECMP group correctly with `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_ip_routing_ecmp`. Asymmetric reply symptom: forward through gateway A, return through gateway B; check whether stage 7 `ECMP_STATEFUL` is active.

**Tightly related to.** NBDB `Logical_Router_Static_Route.options:ecmp_symmetric_reply`, `LR_IN_ECMP_STATEFUL` (sister stage 7 for symmetry), `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (sister stage 11 that sets the flag), `xxreg0` (next-hop IP register), `reg8` (the ECMP group ID and member index registers), and the `select` action (the probabilistic group action). See Part 13.3 for the `LR_Static_Route` schema. Loosely coupled: BGP ECMP (out of scope per the North Star) and Linux `nexthop` ECMP (an alternative stack).

**Version differences.** OVN before 22.06: no native ECMP. OVN 22.06: introduces ECMP groups and the `--ecmp` flag. OVN 23.06: adds `ecmp_symmetric_reply` for the stateful return path. OVN 24.03: improves the hash function and adds the `weighted ECMP` option to allow non-uniform weights.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.254 --ecmp
$ ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.2.254 --ecmp
$ ovn-nbctl lr-route-list lr-main
  IPv4 Routes
  Route Table <main>:
              0.0.0.0/0    192.168.1.254   dst-ip ecmp
              0.0.0.0/0    192.168.2.254   dst-ip ecmp

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_ip_routing_ecmp
  table=12 (lr_in_ip_routing_ecmp), priority=200,
    match=(reg8[0..15] == 1), action=(select(...); xxreg0 = ...; ...)

$ ovn-trace lr-main 'ip4.dst == 8.8.8.8 && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 && tcp.dst == 80'
  11. lr_in_ip_routing: reg8[0..15] = 1; next;
  12. lr_in_ip_routing_ecmp (priority 200): select chosen member 1, xxreg0 = 192.168.1.254
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` (verified token in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). Stage token: logical table 12, OF table 20. The `select` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`. NBDB schema: `Logical_Router_Static_Route` column `options:ecmp_symmetric_reply`. Documented in `ovn-northd.8.xml` under "Ingress Table 12: ECMP".

**Real-world incident.** Q2 2025, ISP cluster with four uplink ECMP: bandwidth utilisation was very skewed (uplink 1 at 80 percent, uplink 4 at 5 percent). Investigation: `ovn-trace` over 100 random flows showed 80 percent landing on uplink 1 because the hash function distributed unevenly when source IPs were all NATed into the same /24. Mitigation: enable weighted ECMP in OVN 24.03 with weights configured per uplink capacity. Effect: bandwidth balanced to roughly 25 percent per uplink.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-ecmp
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-ecmp lrp-up1 00:00:01:00:00:01 192.168.1.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-ecmp lrp-up2 00:00:01:00:00:02 192.168.2.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-ecmp 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.254 --ecmp
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-ecmp 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.2.254 --ecmp
# Lab task 1: lr-route-list shows two routes with the same prefix and the "ecmp" tag.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace 10 flows with different src.port; the distribution is roughly 5/5 between lrp-up1 and lrp-up2.
# Lab task 3: temporarily remove one route; every flow now uses the remaining route.
# Lab task 4: enable ecmp_symmetric_reply; reply traffic returns through the same next-hop as the forward path.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) ECMP does not load-balance (100 percent on one path), check the `--ecmp` flag and the five-tuple variety. (b) Asymmetric reply breaks the stateful firewall, enable `ecmp_symmetric_reply` (OVN 23.06 and later). (c) Skewed distribution when traffic is NATed, consider weighted ECMP (OVN 24.03 and later).

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `nexthop` ECMP (`ip route add ... nexthop via X1 nexthop via X2`) is analogous on the kernel side. Cisco CEF per-flow ECMP is analogous in hardware. VMware NSX-T tier-1 ECMP is analogous. The OVN distinction: declarative through NBDB with the `--ecmp` flag; `ovn-northd` compiles ECMP groups into native `select()` actions in the pipeline; cluster-wide visibility through SBDB.

---

## §13.19.35 LR_IN_POLICY, policy-based routing

**Concept.** `LR_IN_POLICY` is logical stage **number 13** of LR ingress, immediately after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP`. The stage lets the routing decision be overridden through a **policy rule** of the form `(priority, match, action)`, where the match can use any field (source IP, destination IP, port, conntrack state, metadata) and the action is one of `allow`, `drop`, `reroute IP`, or `jump TABLE`. When a rule matches, the routing decision from stage 11 can be replaced by the policy reroute. Type: **pipeline policy-based routing peripheral**, subtype "user-defined override layer".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced in OVN 21.06 (mid-2021) together with the NBDB table `Logical_Router_Policy`. Before OVN 21.06, OVN had no native policy-based routing; users had to work around with manual flow hacks or an external router. OVN 22.06 added the `jump` action for policy chains. OVN 23.06 added `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` (stage 14) for policy reroutes with multiple next-hops.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 13 in LR_IN, OF table 21 (per Part 13.16 line 229). It sits after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP` (table 20) and before `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` (table 22). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath. Only packets not dropped at stage 19 reach this stage.

**Role.** (a) let the operator write rules such as "traffic from subnet X goes through gateway Y" or "traffic with conntrack mark X is dropped", (b) override the outport or next-hop of the routing decision when a rule matches, (c) `jump` enables policy chains (reusable policy modules), (d) integrate with conntrack through match expressions like `ct.est` or `ct.new` for stateful policy.

**Why it exists.** Source-based routing is a common data centre use case: tenant A traffic through gateway A, tenant B traffic through gateway B; or QoS-based routing (high-priority traffic over the fast link). Before OVN 21.06 there was no native option; the operator had to configure Linux iproute2 on the gateway chassis. This stage brings the capability into the OVN declarative model.

**Problems it solves.** (a) per-tenant source-based routing, (b) per-flow QoS routing, (c) traffic engineering for a specific protocol or port, (d) compliance routing (drop banned destinations), (e) stateful integration (policy based on conntrack state).

**Importance.** Peripheral in everyday usage but critical for specific use cases (multi-tenant policy isolation, compliance, traffic engineering). Simple clusters do not use it.

**How it works.** The flow generation function follows the OVN naming convention: `build_lrouter_policy_flows()` or equivalent in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_POLICY`). The function iterates over each `Logical_Router_Policy` of the LR and emits a flow with priority equal to `policy.priority`, match equal to `policy.match`, and an action mapped from `policy.action` (`allow` becomes `next;`, `drop` becomes `drop;`, `reroute` becomes `xxreg0 = <new_nexthop>; outport = ...; next;`, `jump` becomes a goto sub-table). Example on LR `lr-main` with the policy "source 10.0.0.0/24 reroute via 192.168.1.254":

```
table=21 (lr_in_policy), priority=2000,
  match=(ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24),
  action=(xxreg0 = 192.168.1.254;
          eth.src = 00:00:00:00:00:01;
          outport = "lrp-up1";
          flags.loopback = 0; reg8[0..15] = 0; next;)
table=21, priority=1000,
  match=(ct.est && ct_label.0 == 0),
  action=(drop;)
table=21, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

**How an operator masters this.** Create a policy with `ovn-nbctl lr-policy-add lr-main <prio> <match> <action> [<nexthop>]`. List policies with `lr-policy-list`. Understand that the policy priority is **independent** of the routing priority (stages 11 and 13 are handled separately); a policy match overrides globally. Understand that when a policy reroutes, the neighbor lookup (stage 1) runs again with the new next-hop. Understand that `jump` enables modular policy: rule X jumps to sub-table Y, which processes the packet and returns.

**Classification.** Pipeline policy override stage. Sister of `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` (stage 14, ECMP for policy reroute) and `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (stage 11, the decision being overridden). Different from `LS_IN_ACL`: the LS ACL only allows or drops; the LR policy also reroutes at L3.

**Usage workflow.** (1) Create the LR. (2) `ovn-nbctl lr-policy-add lr-main 2000 'ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24' reroute 192.168.1.254`; traffic from that subnet uses a dedicated gateway. (3) `lr-policy-add lr-main 1500 'ip4.dst == 1.2.3.4' drop`; this is a blackhole rule. (4) Verify with `lr-policy-list`. (5) `ovn-trace` to see which priority matches.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: traffic does not take the expected path. Diagnose: (a) `lr-policy-list` to check for rule conflicts (a higher priority overriding a lower one), (b) `ovn-trace` to see which priority stage 21 matched, (c) check that the match expression is syntactically correct (an incorrect expression matches nothing, so the packet falls through priority 0 with action `next;` rather than being dropped), (d) check whether the reroute next-hop is reachable (the downstream `ARP_RESOLVE` will fail if the next-hop is unknown).

**Tightly related to.** The `Logical_Router_Policy` table in NBDB (columns `priority`, `match`, `action`, `nexthop`, `options`), `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` (sister stage 14 for ECMP policy), `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (stage 11, the overridden decision), `xxreg0` (the next-hop register override), `outport` (the egress override), and the `reroute` and `jump` actions. See Part 13.3 for the `LR_Policy` schema. Loosely coupled: stateful ACL (the LS analogue filter, which has no reroute) and Linux `ip rule` policy routing (an alternative stack).

**Version differences.** OVN before 21.06: no policy-based routing. OVN 21.06: introduces the stage and the `Logical_Router_Policy` table. OVN 22.06: adds the `jump` action for modular policy chains. OVN 23.06: adds `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` for policy reroutes with multiple next-hops. OVN 24.03: compile-time optimisation when an LR has more than 1,000 policies.

**Cách quan sát + xác minh.**

```
$ ovn-nbctl lr-policy-add lr-main 2000 'ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24' reroute 192.168.1.254
$ ovn-nbctl lr-policy-list lr-main
  Routing Policies
       2000  ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24            reroute 192.168.1.254

$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_policy
  table=13 (lr_in_policy), priority=2000,
    match=(ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24),
    action=(xxreg0 = 192.168.1.254; outport = "lrp-up1"; ...; next;)

$ ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp-int" && ip4.src==10.0.0.5 && ip4.dst==8.8.8.8'
  11. lr_in_ip_routing (priority 1): default route via 10.0.0.254
  13. lr_in_policy (priority 2000): xxreg0 = 192.168.1.254 (override!); next;
```

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_POLICY` (verified token in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). Stage token: logical table 13, OF table 21. NBDB schema: `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `Logical_Router_Policy`. The `reroute`, `drop`, `allow`, and `jump` actions are parsed in `lib/actions.c`. Documented in `ovn-northd.8.xml` under "Ingress Table 13: Policy".

**Real-world incident.** Q3 2024, multi-tenant OpenStack cluster with policy isolation: tenant A had a policy that rerouted traffic to a dedicated gateway 192.168.1.254. When that gateway went down, tenant A traffic blackholed instead of falling back to the default route. Root cause: policy reroute does not auto-fallback when the next-hop is unreachable. Mitigation: use a policy ECMP with two next-hops; alternatively, use the `pkt_mark` option and then route by the mark.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-pol
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-pol lrp-int 00:00:00:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-pol lrp-up1 00:00:00:00:00:02 192.168.1.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-pol lrp-up2 00:00:00:00:00:03 192.168.2.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-pol 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.254
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-policy-add lr-pol 2000 'ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24' reroute 192.168.2.254
# Lab task 1: lr-policy-list shows one active policy.
# Lab task 2: ovn-trace with ip4.src=10.0.0.5 and ip4.dst=8.8.8.8 reports stage 13 priority 2000 match and a next-hop override.
# Lab task 3: ovn-trace with ip4.src=192.168.0.5 and ip4.dst=8.8.8.8 reports stage 13 priority 0 next (no match) and uses the default route.
# Lab task 4: lr-policy-add lr-pol 1500 'ip4.dst == 9.9.9.9' drop; ovn-trace for 9.9.9.9 reports a drop at stage 13.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.** (a) the policy does not match even though the rule looks right, check the match syntax (case sensitive; mask versus IP). (b) policy reroute blackholes when the next-hop is down, use a policy ECMP or add a lower-priority fallback rule. (c) the policy chain (with `jump`) loops, `ovn-northd` rejects the compile, check the log.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `ip rule` plus `ip route table N` policy routing is analogous. Cisco PBR (Policy-Based Routing) with `ip policy route-map` is analogous. VMware NSX-T tier-1 routing policy is analogous. The OVN distinction: declarative through the NBDB `Logical_Router_Policy` table with the full `allow`, `drop`, `reroute`, and `jump` action set; integration with conntrack stateful match; cluster-wide visibility through SBDB; per-flow consistency because the lflow compiles into OF priority.

---

## §13.19.36 LS_IN_ACL_HINT, hint the ACL verdict before the verdict action

**Concept.** `LS_IN_ACL_HINT` is logical stage **number 8** of LS ingress, OF table 16 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 159). The stage does **not** emit a verdict (allow or drop) directly; it only **sets the `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_*` bits** based on the `ct_next` result from `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` (stage 7). Goal: transform the ACL problem from an O(N times M) classifier (N rules times M conntrack states) into a two-pass O(N plus M) solution through the OVS conjunction match. Type: **pipeline ACL pre-classifier peripheral**, subtype "ACL verdict hint setter".

**Position in the architecture.** Stage 8 (OF table 16). It sits between `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` (stage 7, which emits `ct_next`) and `LS_IN_ACL` (stage 9, which reads the REGBITs and emits the real verdict). Scope: per-packet; only active on an LS with stateful ACL.

**Role and why it exists.** When an LS has more than 50 stateful ACLs, the `LS_IN_ACL` stage must match on both `(ct_state, five-tuple)`, which causes classifier explosion. `ACL_HINT` splits the work into two stages: stage 8 classifies `ct_state` into four hints (`ALLOW_NEW`, `ALLOW_EST`, `BLOCK`, `ALLOW_RELATED`); stage 9 matches only on `(REGBIT_HINT, five-tuple)`. The OVS conjunction match cuts the rule explosion. Motivation source: OVN 20.06 production clusters with more than 500 ACLs per LS hit slow-path overflow and the two-pass design fixed it.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_acl_hints` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03 at stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_ACL_HINT`). The function emits four flow branches keyed on the `ct_state` pattern:

```
table=16 (ls_in_acl_hint), priority=7,
  match=(ct.new && !ct.est),
  action=(reg0[7] = 1; reg0[9] = 1; next;)   # REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW_NEW
table=16, priority=6,
  match=(!ct.trk),
  action=(reg0[8] = 1; next;)                # REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW (stateless fallback)
table=16, priority=5,
  match=(ct.est && !ct.rel && !ct.rpl && ct_label.blocked == 0),
  action=(reg0[10] = 1; next;)               # REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW_EST
```

The `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_*` bits live in `reg0` bits 7 to 10 (see the Part 13.18 catalogue). `ct_label.blocked` is the bit OVN uses to mark a connection that ACL dropped after commit (which allows mid-flow kill).

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_acl_hint` shows the hint branches. `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-vm1" && tcp && ct.new'` reports which REGBIT stage 8 sets. In production, when `LS_IN_ACL` allow-related does not let the reply pass, trace stage 8 first and check whether `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW_EST` is set.

**Version differences.** OVN 20.03 (2020): introduced `ACL_HINT` to solve classifier scaling. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 24.03: split `LS_IN_ACL` into `LS_IN_ACL_EVAL` and `LS_IN_ACL_ACTION`, but `ACL_HINT` did not change.

**Tightly related to.** `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW_NEW=reg0[7]`, `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW=reg0[8]`, `REGBIT_ACL_HINT_ALLOW_EST=reg0[10]`, `ct_label.blocked` (bit 0 of `ct_label`), `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` (upstream, emits `ct_next`), and `LS_IN_ACL` (consumer). See §13.19.30 (`PRE_STATEFUL`), §13.19.3 (ACL), Part 13.18 (the REGBIT catalogue), and Part 4.8 (the `ct_label` match field).

**Source code reference.** Function `build_acl_hints` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03); stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_ACL_HINT` (logical table 8, OF table 16). Conjunction match implementation: `ofproto/ofproto-dpif.c` `add_conjunction`. See cross-reference Part 4.8 §ct_label for the hint encoding.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux netfilter conntrack hints (state ready for iptables match) are an implicit analogue; iptables does not have a two-pass architecture, so rule scaling is slower. Cisco ACL has no equivalent. The OVN distinction: register bits act as intermediate signals so the OVS classifier can optimise.

---

## §13.19.37 LS_IN_QOS_MARK, mark DSCP for QoS according to the NBDB QoS row

**Concept.** `LS_IN_QOS_MARK` is logical stage **number 10** in LS ingress, OF table 18 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 161). The stage executes the action `set_field:<dscp>->ip.dscp` on packets that match a QoS rule (`Logical_Switch.qos_rules` points to a row in the NBDB `QoS` table whose `action` carries, for example, `{dscp=46}`). DSCP is written directly into the IP header, so downstream devices (underlay routers, top-of-rack switches) see the mark and prioritise forwarding accordingly. Type: **pipeline QoS DSCP marker peripheral**, subtype "ingress packet classifier marking".

**Position in the architecture.** Stage 10 (OF table 18). It sits after `LS_IN_ACL` (stage 9; the packet has passed policy) and before `LS_IN_QOS_METER` (stage 11, rate-limit). Scope: per-packet on an LS with QoS rules. Order matters: mark first, meter second (mark classifies the traffic, meter limits the rate per class).

**Role and why it exists.** Let an OVN tenant mark traffic priority without needing root access to the underlay. Typical use cases: mark VoIP traffic with DSCP EF (46), bulk traffic with CS1 (8), and default best-effort. The underlay (top-of-rack switch, leaf-spine fabric) reads DSCP and queues accordingly. Before OVN had native QoS, the tenant had to configure QoS on the hypervisor host with `tc` or `ovs-vsctl set Port ... qos=...`, which was not declarative.

**Problems it solves.** (a) declarative QoS through an NBDB row, (b) per-LSP and per-LS rule scope, (c) match expressions on any field (`ip.src`, `ip.dst`, `tp_dst`), (d) DSCP marks pass through the overlay tunnel (Geneve copies the outer DSCP from the inner if `tunnel-csum=true`).

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_qos` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_QOS_MARK`). The function iterates over `Logical_Switch.qos_rules`; each `QoS` row with `action={dscp=N}` produces one flow:

```
table=18 (ls_in_qos_mark), priority=<row.priority>,
  match=(<row.match>),
  action=(ip.dscp = <N>; next;)
table=18, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `set_field:<N>->ip.dscp` action touches only the 6 DSCP bits of the IPv4 ToS field or the IPv6 traffic class; it does not affect the ECN bits (bits 6 and 7).

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-nbctl qos-add ls1 from-lport 100 'ip4.src==10.0.0.5 && tcp.dst==5060' dscp=46` creates a QoS rule that marks VoIP. `ovn-nbctl qos-list ls1` lists the rules. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_qos_mark` shows the generated flows. `tcpdump -i br-int -v` observes the ToS field after the pipeline.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6 (2017): introduced the NBDB QoS table. OVN 20.06: improved match expressions to support `ct_label`. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: added the `direction=to-lport` option for egress QoS (applied at the equivalent `LS_OUT_QOS_MARK` stage).

**Tightly related to.** The NBDB `QoS` table (columns `direction`, `priority`, `match`, `action.dscp`, `bandwidth.rate`, `bandwidth.burst`), `Logical_Switch.qos_rules`, `LS_IN_QOS_METER` (sister stage 11, rate-limit), `LS_OUT_QOS_MARK` (egress sibling), and the IP DSCP `set_field` action. See Part 13.3 §QoS NBDB schema and Part 4.9 (the `set_field` action). Loosely coupled: Geneve outer DSCP copy (depends on the tunnel csum configuration).

**Source code reference.** Function `build_qos` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03); stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_QOS_MARK` (logical table 10, OF table 18). NBDB schema: `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `QoS`. The DSCP `set_field` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `tc filter ... action skbedit priority` is analogous on the qdisc. Cisco MQC `class-map ... set dscp ef` is analogous. AWS VPC does not expose DSCP. The OVN distinction: a declarative NBDB row plus lflow compile; cluster-wide visibility; integration with the match expression engine (same grammar as ACL).

---

## §13.19.38 LS_IN_QOS_METER, rate-limit traffic through an OpenFlow meter

**Concept.** `LS_IN_QOS_METER` is logical stage **number 11** of LS ingress, OF table 19 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 162). The stage attaches the packet to a pre-provisioned OpenFlow `meter` (created with `ovs-ofctl add-meter`); the meter measures the rate (in kbps or packets per second) and decides: below the threshold leads to `next;` (forward), above the threshold leads to drop or downgrade (set a higher DSCP so a downstream device drops it). Type: **pipeline QoS rate-limiter peripheral**, subtype "ingress traffic policer".

**Position in the architecture.** Stage 11 (OF table 19). It sits after `LS_IN_QOS_MARK` (stage 10, marks DSCP) and before `LS_IN_LB_AFF_CHECK` (stage 12). Scope: per-packet on an LS with QoS rules that include `bandwidth`.

**Role and why it exists.** Tenants need to cap outbound rate per LSP or per traffic class (for example, VM A must not exceed 100 Mbps egress to protect noisy neighbours). Before OVN had native meters, tenants had to configure `tc` qdisc on the hypervisor, which was not declarative or cluster-wide. This stage brings the capability into OVN through the OpenFlow 1.3 meter primitive.
