**Problems it solves.** (a) declarative per-LSP rate limit, (b) per traffic class limit (combined with `QOS_MARK`), (c) cluster-wide consistency (NBDB compiles to lflow; `ovn-controller` installs the meter through `ovs-ofctl` per chassis), (d) drop or downgrade DSCP when the threshold is exceeded (configurable).

**How it works.** Same family flow generation function as `build_qos` (stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_QOS_METER` on branch-22.03). The function reads the `QoS.bandwidth` map (`rate`, `burst`), creates a meter ID per QoS row, and emits a flow:

```
table=19 (ls_in_qos_meter), priority=<row.priority>,
  match=(<row.match>),
  action=(set_meter(<rate_kbps>, <burst_kb>); next;)
table=19, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `set_meter` action (an OVN extension; the OF 1.3 native form is `meter:N`) provisions the meter on demand; `ovn-controller` installs the meter with `ovs-ofctl add-meter br-int meter=N kbps band=type=drop rate=R burst_size=B` before installing the referencing flow.

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-nbctl qos-add ls1 from-lport 100 'ip4.src==10.0.0.5' rate=10000 burst=1000` (a 10 Mbps cap with a 1 MB burst). `ovs-ofctl -O OpenFlow13 dump-meters br-int` shows the installed meters. `ovs-ofctl -O OpenFlow13 meter-stats br-int meter=<id>` shows packet and byte counts plus the drop count when traffic exceeds the rate.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6 (2017): introduced `QoS.bandwidth`. OVN 20.06: added a dedicated `meter` option for ACL log rate-limit (a different stage). OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: supports the `pktps` (packets per second) meter unit in addition to `kbps`. OVN 24.09: improved meter ID allocation to avoid cluster-wide conflicts.

**Tightly related to.** NBDB `QoS.bandwidth.rate` and `bandwidth.burst`, the NBDB `Meter` table (a separate table for ACL log rate-limit), `LS_IN_QOS_MARK` (sister stage 10), `LS_OUT_QOS_METER` (egress sibling), the `set_meter` action, and the OpenFlow 1.3 meter primitive. See Part 13.3 §QoS and Parts 3.5 and 3.6 (the OpenFlow meter MOD message). Loosely coupled: Linux `tc tbf` (token bucket filter) is the analogous underlying primitive.

**Source code reference.** Function `build_qos` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03) handles both `QOS_MARK` and `QOS_METER`; stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_QOS_METER` (logical table 11, OF table 19). The `set_meter` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`. Meter installation at runtime: `controller/ofctrl.c`.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `tc tbf` token bucket filter is analogous (per interface). Cisco policer `police 10000 conform-action transmit exceed-action drop` is analogous. AWS VPC instance bandwidth limit per instance type is analogous (the meter API is not exposed). The OVN distinction: declarative through NBDB `QoS.bandwidth` plus cluster-wide installation by `ovn-controller`; per-LSP and per-traffic-class scope instead of per physical port.

---

## §13.19.39 LS_IN_STATEFUL, commit a conntrack entry for a new connection

**Concept.** `LS_IN_STATEFUL` is logical stage **number 17** of LS ingress, OF table 25 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 168). The stage is the **final stateful point** of the LS ingress pipeline: when `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT=reg0[1]` has been set by `LS_IN_ACL` (allow-related) or by `LS_IN_LB` (DNAT commit), this stage emits `ct(commit, zone=reg13, exec(...))` to write the connection into the kernel conntrack table, so the reply path is automatically allowed. Type: **pipeline stateful committer peripheral**, subtype "conntrack final commit".

**Position in the architecture.** Stage 17 (OF table 25). It sits after `LS_IN_PRE_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_HAIRPIN`, and `LS_IN_NAT_HAIRPIN` (stages 13 to 16) and before `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (stage 18). Scope: per-packet on an LS with stateful ACL (`allow-related`) or LB.

**Role and why it exists.** OVN's `allow-related` ACL must commit the connection so that the reply path (which does not match an explicit allow rule because the rule only allows the forward direction) is automatically permitted through the match `ct.est && !ct.rpl`. This stage separates the commit from `LS_IN_ACL` to make sure the commit happens **after** every other stateful decision (LB DNAT, hairpin SNAT) has completed, avoiding a wrong-tuple commit. Splitting the stage also lets the `ct_label.blocked` flag be written together with the commit (encoding the policy denied state into the label for downstream stages to reference).

**Problems it solves.** (a) a single commit point instead of commits scattered across multiple stages, (b) commit after DNAT and SNAT have applied (the five-tuple matches the external connection), (c) commit together with `ct_label` to mark the policy decision, (d) skip when `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT` is not set (stateless flow).

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_stateful` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_STATEFUL`). The function emits two main flow groups: a commit group for ACL allow-related and a commit group for LB DNAT:

```
table=25 (ls_in_stateful), priority=100,
  match=(reg0[1] == 1),                              # REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT
  action=(ct_commit { ct_label.blocked = 0; }; next;)
table=25, priority=100,
  match=(reg0[2] == 1),                              # REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT
  action=(ct_lb_mark;)
table=25, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `ct_commit { ct_label.blocked = 0; }` syntax expands at the OVN level into `ct(commit, zone=NXM_NX_REG13[0..15], exec(set_field:0/0x1->ct_label))` in the OF rule. `ct_lb_mark` commits the DNAT mapping into the LB conntrack zone (separate from the ACL zone).

**Usage workflow and observation.** Create an LS with an `allow-related` ACL and an LB. Send a new connection; `ovn-trace` reports that stage 17 emits `ct_commit`. `conntrack -L -z <zone_id>` (the Linux conntrack tool) verifies the new entry. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=25` shows the rules and `n_packets`.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6 (2017): introduced a simple stateful stage. OVN 2.7: added `ct_label.blocked` to let ACL kill a connection mid-flow. OVN 21.06: added the `ct_lb_mark` action to replace the old `ct_lb`. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 24.03: compile-time optimisation when an LS has more than 1,000 LB endpoints.

**Tightly related to.** `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_COMMIT=reg0[1]`, `REGBIT_CONNTRACK_NAT=reg0[2]`, the `ct_label.blocked` bit, `reg13` (the conntrack zone), the `ct_commit` and `ct_lb_mark` actions, `LS_IN_ACL` (upstream, sets the REGBIT for allow-related), `LS_IN_LB` (upstream, sets the REGBIT for DNAT), and `LS_OUT_STATEFUL` (egress sibling). See Part 13.18 (the REGBIT catalogue), Part 9.24 (OVS conntrack), and Part 4.8 (`ct_label`).

**Source code reference.** Function `build_stateful` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03); stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_STATEFUL` (logical table 17, OF table 25). The `ct_commit` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c` by `parse_CT_COMMIT_V2`. Conntrack runtime: `lib/conntrack.c` (OVS kernel and userspace).

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-m conntrack --ctstate NEW -j ACCEPT` plus the implicit kernel commit is analogous; iptables does not split commit into a dedicated stage, and the kernel commits automatically when the packet exits the chain. Cisco ASA stateful inspection commit is implicit. The OVN distinction: an explicit commit stage allows fine-grained policy (choose to commit or not, attach a label) and separates the ACL zone from the LB zone to support hairpin.

---

## §13.19.40 LS_IN_DNS_LOOKUP, answer DNS queries natively in the pipeline

**Concept.** `LS_IN_DNS_LOOKUP` is logical stage **number 21** of LS ingress, OF table 29 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 172). The stage matches DNS query packets (UDP destination port 53) and calls the `dns_lookup()` action to look the fqdn up in the NBDB `DNS_Records` map (which maps fqdn to IP); on a hit, the action builds the DNS response payload into the current packet and sets `REGBIT_DNS_LOOKUP_RESULT=reg0[4]=1` so the downstream stage emits the reply. Type: **pipeline DNS server inline peripheral**, subtype "DNS query interceptor".

**Position in the architecture.** Stage 21 (OF table 29). It sits after `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE` (stage 20) and before `LS_IN_EXTERNAL_PORT` (stage 22, or its equivalent). Scope: per-packet (DNS UDP port 53 only), per-LS that has `Logical_Switch.dns_records` configured.

**Role and why it exists.** Cloud tenants need DNS resolution for service discovery (`db.tenant1.local` to `10.0.0.10`) without deploying an external dnsmasq and without forwarding DNS queries upstream. OVN intercepts DNS queries natively in the pipeline, reducing latency from milliseconds (a network round-trip to dnsmasq) to microseconds (an in-kernel datapath response). Before native DNS, tenants had to configure dnsmasq on each hypervisor manually.

**Problems it solves.** (a) native DNS resolution with no external daemon, (b) declarative through NBDB `DNS_Records`, (c) per-LS scope (each tenant has its own records), (d) low latency because the work is done in-kernel or in OVS userspace, (e) transparent fallback (if the fqdn does not match local records, the packet passes through to upstream DNS).

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lswitch_dns_lookup_and_response` in `northd/northd.c` (verified on branch-22.03; stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_DNS_LOOKUP`; the function follows the same family as the `DHCP_OPTIONS` and `DHCP_RESPONSE` pattern). The function emits two branches:

```
table=29 (ls_in_dns_lookup), priority=100,
  match=(udp.dst == 53),
  action=(reg0[4] = dns_lookup(); next;)
table=29, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `dns_lookup` action is a **controller action**: the packet is upcalled to `ovn-controller` and `pinctrl_handle_dns_lookup()` (`controller/pinctrl.c`) handles it; the handler parses the DNS query qname, looks it up in the `DNS_Records` cache synced from SBDB, and on a hit builds the DNS response payload (the RR section) overwriting the packet payload and returning REGBIT=1; on a miss it returns REGBIT=0 (the packet passes through). The next stage `LS_IN_DNS_RESPONSE` (stage 22, outside the scope of this keyword) checks the REGBIT and emits the reply.

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-nbctl set Logical_Switch ls1 dns_records=@dns1 -- --id=@dns1 create DNS records='{"web.local"="10.0.0.5"}'` provisions the DNS row. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_dns_lookup` shows the flow. A VM in the LS runs `dig @<DNS server VIP> web.local` and the reply arrives in less than 1 millisecond. `tail -f /var/log/ovn/ovn-controller.log | grep dns_lookup` shows the upcall handler trace.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.8 (2018): introduced native DNS with the `DNS_LOOKUP` and `DNS_RESPONSE` pair. OVN 20.06: added support for reverse DNS (PTR records). OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: supports `DNS.options:server_response_max_size` for large records. OVN 24.09: improved cache invalidation when a `DNS_Records` row is updated.

**Tightly related to.** The NBDB `DNS` table (columns `records` mapping fqdn to IP, `external_ids`), `Logical_Switch.dns_records`, the `dns_lookup` action, `REGBIT_DNS_LOOKUP_RESULT=reg0[4]`, `LS_IN_DNS_RESPONSE` (stage 22 sibling, emits the reply), and `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_dns_lookup`. See Part 13.10 (the OVN native DHCP and DNS deep dive), Part 13.18 (the REGBIT catalogue), and Part 4.9 (controller actions). Loosely coupled: dnsmasq (an alternative external daemon) and the external upstream DNS (the passthrough fallback).

**Source code reference.** Function `build_lswitch_dns_lookup_and_response` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03; emits flows for both `DNS_LOOKUP` and `DNS_RESPONSE`); stage token `S_SWITCH_IN_DNS_LOOKUP` (logical table 21, OF table 29). Action parser: `lib/actions.c` `parse_DNS_LOOKUP`. Runtime handler: `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_dns_lookup` parses the DNS query and builds the response. NBDB schema: `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `DNS`. Documented in `ovn-northd.8.xml` under "Ingress Table 21: DNS Lookup".

**Comparison with other systems.** A dnsmasq daemon is analogous (userspace, per host). CoreDNS in Kubernetes is analogous (cluster-wide pod). AWS Route 53 Resolver is analogous (managed). The OVN distinction: DNS resolution runs directly in the OVN pipeline through a controller upcall, with no separate daemon; declarative through an NBDB DNS row with per-LS scope; cluster-wide visibility through SBDB; very low latency because the handler runs inside the `ovn-controller` process.

---

## §13.19.41 LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE, attach the next-hop MAC to a packet about to leave the LR

**Concept.** `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` is logical stage **number 15** of LR ingress, OF table 23 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 231). The stage is the **final resolve point** of the neighbor handling chain: after `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (stage 1) set `MFF_LOG_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR=reg9[2]` for hit or miss, and after `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (stage 11) set `xxreg0=<next-hop IP>` and `outport=<LRP egress>`, stage 23 sets `eth.dst = <MAC of the next-hop>` from the `MAC_Binding` cache (or from `Static_MAC_Binding`). On a cache hit, set the MAC directly and `next;`; on a miss, leave `eth.dst` unchanged and let `LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST` (stage 19) handle the ARP emit. Type: **pipeline ingress neighbor binding applier peripheral**, subtype "MAC_Binding apply step".

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 15, OF table 23. It sits after `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP` (stage 14, table 22) and before `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` (stage 16, table 24). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath; every packet that already has a routing decision must pass through. The neighbor stage quartet: 1 (LOOKUP), 2 (LEARN), 15 (RESOLVE), 19 (REQUEST). Splitting these four stages keeps the operations idempotent and provides policy injection points in between.

**Role and why it exists.** Every packet exiting the LR needs the correct `eth.dst`, which is the MAC of the next-hop (the LSP for the next-hop subnet, or the external gateway MAC). If routing carried the MAC, every route entry would have to embed it, which would bloat the FIB. Splitting RESOLVE out: routing only needs to know the next-hop IP and the outport; the MAC binding lookup is separate. When `MAC_Binding` updates (gateway failover, ARP refresh), only the SBDB row needs to update; the `IP_ROUTING` lflow does not have to recompile.

**Problems it solves.** (a) decouple the FIB from the MAC binding (the FIB is stable, the MAC binding is volatile), (b) let `Static_MAC_Binding` override dynamic learning, (c) consolidate the "rewrite `eth.dst`" point into a single stage so cookie-based debugging is easier, (d) on a miss, the REGBIT lets stage 19 decide whether to emit the ARP request, with no re-emit if one was just sent (pinctrl-level rate-limiting).

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lrouter_arp_resolve_flows` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_ARP_RESOLVE`). The function emits three flow groups: (a) priority 100 match `outport == "lrp1" && reg0 == 10.0.0.10` (the next-hop IP is known) and action `eth.dst = aa:bb:cc:00:00:10; next;`; (b) priority 50 match `outport == "lrp1" && eth.dst == 00:00:00:00:00:00` (the MAC is not set) and action `get_arp(outport, reg0); next;` (a controller action that looks up the cache); (c) priority 0 catch-all `next;`. A concrete example:

```
table=23 (lr_in_arp_resolve), priority=100,
  match=(outport == "lrp-uplink" && reg0 == 10.0.0.254),
  action=(eth.dst = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01; next;)
table=23, priority=50,
  match=(ip4 && outport == "lrp-uplink"),
  action=(get_arp(outport, reg0); next;)
table=23, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `get_arp` action upcalls `pinctrl_handle_get_arp` to look up `MAC_Binding` at runtime; on a hit, the controller installs a new priority 100 flow for that IP (lazy population).

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_in_arp_resolve` shows the flows for the LR. `ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp1" && ip4.src==10.0.0.5 && ip4.dst==192.168.1.5'` shows stage 23 setting `eth.dst`. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=23 | grep n_packets` shows the traffic count. When the gateway MAC changes (vRouter swap), monitor the SBDB `MAC_Binding` row update and the lflow recompile latency.

**Version differences.** OVN before 21.03: the stage performed both lookup and resolve combined. OVN 21.03: split LOOKUP and LEARN out first; `ARP_RESOLVE` retained the MAC-apply role. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: improved the priority 50 catch-all so it does not match packets already pre-populated by `Static_MAC_Binding`. OVN 24.03: added multi-MAC binding support for ECMP with different next-hops.

**Tightly related to.** SBDB `MAC_Binding` (input data), NBDB `Static_MAC_Binding` (config-driven), `xxreg0` (the next-hop IP register set by routing), `eth.dst` field rewrite, `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` (stage 1, sets the REGBIT), `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (stage 2, inserts `MAC_Binding`), `LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST` (stage 19, emits ARP on a miss), and `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` (downstream consumer that needs the correct `eth.dst`). See Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.3 (the `MAC_Binding` deep dive), Part 13.18 (the REGBIT catalogue), and Part 13.16 line 231.

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lrouter_arp_resolve_flows` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` (logical table 15, OF table 23). The `get_arp` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c` by `parse_GET_ARP`. Runtime handler: `controller/pinctrl.c` `pinctrl_handle_get_arp_or_nd`. SBDB schema: `MAC_Binding`.

**Real-world incidents and common failures.** Symptom: traffic exiting the LR drops after a gateway failover. Cause: the `MAC_Binding` cache is stale (the old MAC of the previously active gateway chassis, the new gateway has not been learned). Diagnose: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=23 | grep <gateway_ip>` shows the old MAC; clean up with `ovn-sbctl destroy MAC_Binding <uuid>` and pre-populate `Static_MAC_Binding`. Common pitfall: a route points to a next-hop outside the LRP subnet, ARP lookup fails forever, and every packet stays stuck at priority 50.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux kernel rewriting the L2 destination through the `neigh` stack is analogous. Cisco IOS ARP cache lookup before transmit is analogous. The OVN distinction: stage 23 is fully separated for the rewrite, and cluster-wide `MAC_Binding` through SBDB lets every chassis share the same cache instead of learning per-host.

---

## §13.19.42 LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST, emit an ARP or ND request when MAC_Binding misses after resolve

**Concept.** `LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST` is logical stage **number 19** of LR ingress, OF table 27 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 235). The stage is the **last LR ingress stage**: if `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` (stage 15) leaves the packet without a valid `eth.dst` (still the `00:00:00:00:00:00` placeholder), this stage invokes a controller action to emit an ARP or ND request out the outport to learn the MAC from the next-hop, and typically drops the current packet. Type: **pipeline ingress ARP emitter peripheral**, subtype "neighbor solicitation generator".

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 19, OF table 27, the last stage in LR ingress. It sits after `LR_IN_GW_REDIRECT` (stage 18, table 26) and is the final stage before the packet moves to the egress pipeline (through the `next_pipeline` action). Scope: per-packet without a MAC binding after `ARP_RESOLVE`.

**Role and why it exists.** When the LR needs to forward a packet to a next-hop with an unknown MAC, the system must emit an ARP request (IPv4) or an ND solicitation (IPv6) to learn it. OVN puts the emit into the final ingress stage because: (a) the emit needs the outport to be stable, (b) dropping the current packet avoids buffering inside the datapath, (c) when the reply arrives, `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (stage 2) inserts the `MAC_Binding` for next time.

**Problems it solves.** (a) trigger ARP and ND learning from the data plane, (b) consolidate the emit into a single stage so rate-limiting is easy (to avoid ARP storms), (c) separate from RESOLVE to keep clear semantics: RESOLVE applies the MAC, REQUEST learns the MAC, (d) allow static drop (no emit) through policy.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lrouter_arp_request_flows` in `northd/northd.c` (stage token `S_ROUTER_IN_ARP_REQUEST`). The function emits: (a) priority 100 match `eth.dst == 00:00:00:00:00:00 && ip4` and action `arp { eth.dst = ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff; arp.spa = reg1; arp.tpa = reg0; arp.op = 1; output; }; output;` (build the ARP request from a template and output); (b) priority 100 match `eth.dst == 00:00:00:00:00:00 && ip6` and action `nd_ns { ... }; output;`; (c) priority 0 catch-all `output;`. Example:

```
table=27 (lr_in_arp_request), priority=100,
  match=(eth.dst == 00:00:00:00:00:00 && ip4),
  action=(arp { eth.dst = ff:ff:ff:ff:ff:ff;
                arp.spa = reg1; arp.tpa = reg0;
                arp.op = 1; output; };)
table=27, priority=0, match=(1), action=(output;)
```

The `arp { ... }` action is an OVN compound action: clone the packet, build the ARP frame inside the nested scope, and emit it through the outport. The original packet is then dropped (there is no `output` on the IPv4 path).

**Usage workflow and observation.** When a tenant VM sends the first packet to a next-hop with an unknown MAC, `ovn-trace` reports the packet reaching table 27 and the `arp{}` action emitting. `tcpdump -i br-int -e arp` shows the ARP request. About 1 millisecond later the ARP reply arrives, `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` inserts the `MAC_Binding`, and the second packet passes normally. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=27` shows the `n_packets` emit count.

**Version differences.** OVN before 21.03: the emit logic was embedded in `ARP_RESOLVE`, not split out. OVN 21.03: split the REQUEST stage out. OVN 22.03: stable. OVN 23.06: added `arp { }` rate-limit at pinctrl to avoid storms when many packets wait for the same next-hop. OVN 24.03: added the `ovn-northd:arp_request_drop_threshold` option to drop packets after N retries that fail to learn the MAC.

**Tightly related to.** The compound actions `arp { }` and `nd_ns { }` (parsed in `lib/actions.c`), `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` (sister stage 15), `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR` (consumer when the reply arrives), the SBDB `MAC_Binding`, `xxreg0` (the next-hop IP), and `xxreg1` (the source IP of the LRP). See Part 13.18 (the REGBIT catalogue), Part 13.5 §13.5.1c.3 (`MAC_Binding`), and Part 13.16 line 235. Loosely coupled: external gateway BFD (link liveness); if the gateway is down, the ARP request keeps failing.

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lrouter_arp_request_flows` handles stage `S_ROUTER_IN_ARP_REQUEST` (logical table 19, OF table 27). The compound actions are parsed in `lib/actions.c` by `parse_ARP` and `parse_ND_NS`. Runtime: the emit goes through `controller/ofctrl.c` with no upcall; the action is processed in the datapath through flow cloning.

**Real-world incidents and common failures.** Symptom: a full cluster restart causes an ARP broadcast storm on the external VLAN, the upstream switch rate-limits, and packets drop in batches. Mitigation: pre-populate `Static_MAC_Binding` for the gateway, configure `ovn-northd:arp_request_drop_threshold` to cap retries, and monitor `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=27 | grep n_packets` for spikes. Common pitfall: a route points to a next-hop outside the LRP subnet, the ARP request goes into the void, and the MAC is never learned.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux `arp_solicit` kernel triggering an ARP emit is analogous. Cisco IOS emitting ARP when the forwarding lookup misses the MAC is analogous. The OVN distinction: a fully separated stage 19 plus the emit through a data-plane compound action (no upcall) reduces overhead compared to pinctrl-based learning; cluster-wide `MAC_Binding` through SBDB lets one chassis learn and every chassis benefit.

---

## §13.19.43 LR_OUT_UNDNAT, reverse DNAT for reply packets leaving the router

**Concept.** `LR_OUT_UNDNAT` is logical stage **number 1** of LR egress, OF table 41 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 251). The stage is the **symmetric counterpart of `LR_IN_DNAT`**: when the ingress packet was DNAT'd (rewriting `ip.dst` from a floating IP to an internal IP), the reply packet in the reverse direction must be un-DNAT'd to restore `ip.src` back to the original floating IP before leaving the LR. The stage calls `ct(table=Y, zone=DNAT_zone, nat)` so conntrack recognises the reply packet (through the five-tuple match) and reverses the NAT mapping. Type: **pipeline egress NAT reverser peripheral**, subtype "DNAT reverse stage".

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 1, OF table 41, at the start of the LR egress pipeline. It sits after `LR_OUT_CHECK_DNAT_LOCAL` (stage 0, table 40) and before `LR_OUT_POST_UNDNAT` (stage 2, table 42). Scope: per-packet on an LR egress with at least one DNAT or `dnat_and_snat` NAT entry. NAT family: `LR_IN_UNSNAT` (symmetric for SNAT reverse), `LR_OUT_SNAT` (forward SNAT), and `LR_IN_DNAT` (forward DNAT).

**Role and why it exists.** Floating IP and port-forwarding patterns require connection tracking so the reply is automatically un-NAT'd. If the external client saw a reply with the internal IP (10.x), it would drop because the five-tuple would not match the original connection. The UNDNAT stage maintains the invariant: from outside, the connection appears only through the floating IP and the internal topology does not leak.

**Problems it solves.** (a) reverse DNAT mapping through the conntrack zone, (b) separate reverse from forward (DNAT on ingress, UNDNAT on egress) for clear semantics, (c) keep the DNAT zone distinct from the SNAT zone so hairpin scenarios do not conflict, (d) un-NAT reply packets in the same connection idempotently.

**How it works.** Flow generation functions: `build_lrouter_out_undnat_flow` (per LB) and `build_lrouter_out_undnat_flow_normal_vip` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_OUT_UNDNAT`). The functions emit a priority 100 flow per NAT entry of type `dnat` or `dnat_and_snat`:

```
table=41 (lr_out_undnat), priority=100,
  match=(ip4 && ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 && outport == "lrp-uplink"
         && is_chassis_resident("cr-lrp-uplink")),
  action=(ct_dnat;)
table=41, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `ct_dnat` action with no argument on egress means "reverse DNAT the packet in the current conntrack zone". Conntrack looks the entry up with the original tuple (`10.0.0.5`, ...) and reverses the mapping back to (`192.0.2.10`, ...), rewriting `ip.src`. The `is_chassis_resident()` predicate ensures that only the gateway chassis (the cr port owner) performs UNDNAT, keeping it centralised.

**Usage workflow and observation.** Setup: `ovn-nbctl -- --id=@nat create NAT type=dnat_and_snat external_ip=192.0.2.10 logical_ip=10.0.0.5 -- add Logical_Router lr-main nat @nat`. VM 10.0.0.5 receives an inbound packet (DNAT on ingress) and sends a reply. Trace: `ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp-vm" && ip4.src==10.0.0.5 && ip4.dst==<client>'` shows stage 41 emitting `ct_dnat`. `conntrack -L -z <dnat_zone>` shows the entry. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=41` shows `n_packets`.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6: introduced the DNAT and UNDNAT pair. OVN 20.06: split out `POST_UNDNAT` (stage 2) for policy injection between undnat and snat. OVN 21.06: added hairpin support through `LR_OUT_CHECK_DNAT_LOCAL` (stage 0) to skip undnat when the destination is local. OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 23.06: optimised the `is_chassis_resident` predicate for distributed gateways. OVN 24.03: added `stateless` NAT support that bypasses UNDNAT.

**Tightly related to.** The NBDB `NAT` table (columns `type=dnat` or `dnat_and_snat`, `external_ip`, `logical_ip`), `LR_IN_DNAT` (the corresponding forward path, stage 6), `LR_OUT_POST_UNDNAT` (downstream stage 2), `LR_OUT_CHECK_DNAT_LOCAL` (upstream stage 0, skips for hairpin), the DNAT conntrack zone (column `Logical_Router.options:dnat_force_snat_ip` forces SNAT after UNDNAT), the `ct_dnat` action, the `is_chassis_resident` predicate, and the kernel `nf_nat_module`. See Part 13.3 §NAT (the NAT deep dive), Part 13.5 §port_binding chassisredirect, and Part 13.16 line 251.

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` functions `build_lrouter_out_undnat_flow` and `build_lrouter_out_undnat_flow_normal_vip` handle stage `S_ROUTER_OUT_UNDNAT` (logical table 1, OF table 41). The `ct_dnat` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c` by `parse_CT_DNAT_IN_V2`. Runtime: kernel `nf_nat_module` through the `ct()` hook of the OVS conntrack action.

**Real-world incidents and common failures.** Q1 2025, OpenStack-on-OVN: floating IP reply traffic dropped intermittently after a gateway failover. Cause: the DNAT conntrack zone existed only on the active gateway; after failover, the new gateway had no entry, so the reply was not un-DNAT'd, the client saw the internal IP, and the connection dropped. Mitigation: enable `external_ids:always_learn_from_arp_request=true` to push NAT into SBDB-replicated flows rather than relying only on the kernel conntrack; increase `ovn-controller --conntrack-zone-replication` (where available). Common pitfall: an `is_chassis_resident` predicate mismatch when the cr port migrates means the packet does not match any priority 100 flow and falls through without being un-NAT'd.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-t nat -A POSTROUTING ... -j SNAT` plus conntrack reverse mapping is analogous. Cisco IOS `ip nat outside source` is analogous. The OVN distinction: an explicit stage in the pipeline, declarative through the NBDB NAT row, and separate conntrack zones for DNAT and SNAT so hairpin scenarios run without conflict; the `is_chassis_resident()` predicate ensures centralised execution on a single gateway chassis.

---

## §13.19.44 LR_OUT_DELIVERY, hand the packet over to the egress LRP or patch port

**Concept.** `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` is logical stage **number 6** of LR egress, OF table 46 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 256), the **final stage** of the whole LR pipeline. The stage has a single duty: emit the packet through the physical realisation of the `outport` set by `IP_ROUTING`. If the `outport` is an LRP linked to an LSP (peer is an LSP), the packet is resubmitted into the ingress pipeline of the peer switch (through the `output` action); if the `outport` is an LRP linked to another LR through a patch port, the packet goes through the patch peer; if it is a chassisredirect (cr) port, the packet is output through a Geneve tunnel to the gateway chassis. Type: **pipeline egress final delivery peripheral**, subtype "physical output gate".

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 6, OF table 46, the last stage of LR egress. It sits after `LR_OUT_EGR_LOOP` (stage 5, table 45). After this stage the packet leaves the LR pipeline entirely. Scope: every packet leaving the LR must pass through this stage.

**Role and why it exists.** Splitting "delivery" into its own stage helps because: (a) `IP_ROUTING` only sets the logical outport (LRP name) and does not know the physical realisation; (b) chassisredirect requires tunnel encapsulation that differs from a direct patch port; (c) the emit differs between "LR to next LSP" (must resubmit into ingress LSP) and "LR to external network" (output through the LRP-bound port); (d) the final stage is also the logging and drop-counter point for egress.

**Problems it solves.** (a) separate the logical routing decision from the physical delivery, (b) chassisredirect resolution (LRP local or output through tunnel), (c) support LRP peer being an LSP (resubmit into the LSP pipeline) or a patch port (emit through the peer's table 0), (d) consolidate the logging and drop-counter point for egress.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lrouter_out_delivery` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_OUT_DELIVERY`). The function emits a priority 110 flow per LRP with match `outport == "lrp-name"` and action `output;`. A predicate handles chassisredirect separately:

```
table=46 (lr_out_delivery), priority=110,
  match=(outport == "lrp-vm" && !is_chassis_resident("lrp-vm")),
  action=(drop;)
table=46, priority=100,
  match=(outport == "lrp-vm"),
  action=(output;)
table=46, priority=0, match=(1), action=(drop;)
```

The `output` action here emits the packet through the port linked to the LRP. The controller resolves: LRP, then patch port, then ingress pipeline of the peer LSP (or tunnel encapsulation if the peer LSP is remote). The `!is_chassis_resident()` predicate drops packets that do not belong to the current chassis, which avoids duplicate output across the cluster.

**Usage workflow and observation.** `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-main | grep lr_out_delivery` shows the flow per LRP. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=46` shows `n_packets` per outport. `ovn-trace` typically ends with "output to lrp-name", indicating that the packet reached stage 46 successfully. When the stage drops (priority 0 catch-all), check whether `outport` was set correctly (commonly because `IP_ROUTING` did not match the prefix).

**Version differences.** OVN 2.6: introduced a simple DELIVERY stage (output only). OVN 20.06: added the `is_chassis_resident` predicate for distributed gateways. OVN 21.06: added support for LRPs backed by multiple chassis (`HA_Chassis_Group`). OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 24.03: improved priority handling when an LRP has both `gateway_chassis` and `HA_Chassis_Group` configured.

**Tightly related to.** `Port_Binding.type` = `patch`, `l3gateway`, or `chassisredirect` (the downstream consumer depends on the type), the NBDB `Logical_Router_Port` (defines the LRP), `LR_IN_GW_REDIRECT` (upstream stage 18, decides the chassisredirect path), `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (sets the outport), the `output` action, and the `is_chassis_resident` predicate. See Part 13.5 §port_binding catalogue (8 types), Part 13.16 line 256, and Part 13.5b for the cr port type.

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` function `build_lrouter_out_delivery` handles stage `S_ROUTER_OUT_DELIVERY` (logical table 6, OF table 46). The `output` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c`. Runtime: `controller/physical.c` resolves the `outport` to an OF action (output through the patch peer or tunnel encapsulation with Geneve TLVs).

**Real-world incidents and common failures.** Symptom: traffic exiting the LR drops at stage 46 priority 0. Common causes: (a) `outport` is not set (the route did not match the prefix), (b) `is_chassis_resident` mismatch after an LRP migration (`HA_Chassis_Group` failover has not fully propagated), (c) the LRP peer LSP has been deleted but the route entry is stale. Diagnose: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=46 priority=0 | grep n_packets` shows a spike, meaning packets arrived but did not match. Mitigation: recheck NB NAT and `Logical_Router_Static_Route` consistency, and force `ovn-controller` to resync.

**Comparison with other systems.** The Linux kernel `dev_queue_xmit` final emit point is analogous (per net-device). Cisco IOS `egress interface output` is analogous. The OVN distinction: delivery is split into a logical stage with per-chassis predicates (`is_chassis_resident`), which ensures only one chassis emits in distributed scenarios; the same `output` action covers multiple realisations (patch, l3gateway, cr), with the controller resolving them differently.

---

## §13.19.45 LR_OUT_EGR_LOOP, detect and re-inject packets through chassisredirect

**Concept.** `LR_OUT_EGR_LOOP` (sometimes called the "egress loopback stage") is logical stage **number 5** of LR egress, OF table 45 on `br-int` (per Part 13.16 line 255). The stage has two intertwined duties: (a) detect packets caught in a routing loop back to the same LR (rare, but happens with misconfigured policy routing or with `dnat_and_snat` hairpin) and (b) re-inject the packet back into the ingress pipeline when chassisredirect requires it (when the gateway chassis receives a packet from the tunnel and needs to run the local ingress pipeline rather than re-tunnel). The stage uses `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK=reg9[0]` (see Part 13.18 and Part 13.17) as the flag that marks a packet currently in the loopback round. Type: **pipeline egress loopback gate peripheral**, subtype "hairpin and chassisredirect re-injection".

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 5, OF table 45. It sits after `LR_OUT_POST_SNAT` (stage 4, table 44) and before `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` (stage 6, table 46). Scope: per-packet, especially important for LRs with a distributed gateway (a chassisredirect port) or `dnat_and_snat` hairpin scenarios.

**Role and why it exists.** Two main use cases:

1. **Loop prevention.** When a packet is caught in a routing loop (for example, policy routing redirects the packet back to the same LR), the `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK` bit set on the previous round causes the stage to drop the packet to break the infinite loop.
2. **Chassisredirect re-injection.** When the gateway chassis receives a packet from the tunnel (the packet has already gone through the egress pipeline on the source chassis), it must be re-injected into the local ingress pipeline to run SNAT and DNAT centrally; this stage sets `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK=1` and resubmits to LR ingress table 8.

**Problems it solves.** (a) block infinite routing loops (datapath-level safety), (b) let the gateway chassis run NAT centrally without a second tunnel hop, (c) separate the re-inject logic from `GW_REDIRECT` (ingress stage 18) to keep the semantics clear, (d) consolidate the loopback flag into one bit `reg9[0]` so `ovn-trace` debugging is straightforward.

**How it works.** Flow generation function: `build_lrouter_egress_loopback_flow` (or the equivalent `build_egress_loopback_flow`) in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03; stage token `S_ROUTER_OUT_EGR_LOOP`). The function emits: (a) priority 1000 match `reg9[0] == 1` and action `next;` (already in a loopback round, let it pass to avoid an infinite loop); (b) priority 100 match for the hairpin scenario and action `reg9[0] = 1; next(pipeline=ingress, table=0);`; (c) priority 0 catch-all `next;`. Illustrative example:

```
table=45 (lr_out_egr_loop), priority=1000,
  match=(reg9[0] == 1),
  action=(next;)
table=45, priority=300,
  match=(ip4.dst == 192.0.2.10 && outport == "lrp-uplink"),
  action=(reg9[0] = 1;
          eth.dst <-> eth.src;
          next(pipeline=ingress, table=8);)
table=45, priority=0, match=(1), action=(next;)
```

Function `build_lrouter_egress_loopback_flow` (verified in `northd/northd.c` on branch-22.03) inserts a re-inject flow for each NAT row whose `external_ip` matches a hairpin VIP. The `next(pipeline=ingress, table=N)` action is an OVN extension that allows cross-pipeline dispatch within the same datapath.

**Usage workflow and observation.** Hairpin scenario test: VM A calls the floating IP of VM B (both in the same tenant LR). The packet goes through LR egress toward IP B; stage 45 detects the hairpin, sets `reg9[0]=1`, re-injects into ingress, the DNAT lookup runs, and the packet forwards to B. Trace: `ovn-trace lr-main 'inport=="lrp-A" && ip4.dst==<B-FIP>'` shows two pipeline rounds with the `reg9[0]` bit flipping in between. `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=45` shows `n_packets` for each priority class.

**Version differences.** OVN 2.7: introduced `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK` and the stage. OVN 20.06: extended for `dnat_and_snat` hairpin. OVN 21.06: added hairpin LB support (a separate stage on LS, does not affect LR). OVN 22.03 (the curriculum baseline): stable. OVN 24.03: improved priorities to avoid loops in nested NAT (a hairpin into an LR that has its own DNAT).

**Tightly related to.** `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK=reg9[0]` (defined in `northd/northd.c`, in the same bit family as `REGBIT_PKT_LARGER` and `REGBIT_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR_*`), `LR_IN_GW_REDIRECT` (stage 18, the counterpart for the forward path), `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` (downstream consumer when no loopback occurs), the `next(pipeline=ingress)` cross-dispatch action, and the NBDB `NAT.type=dnat_and_snat` (which triggers the hairpin). See Part 13.18 §REGBIT catalogue line `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK`, Part 13.17 §logical-fields.h, Part 13.16 line 255, and Part 19.0 §multi-chassis hairpin.

**Source code reference.** `northd/northd.c` handles stage `S_ROUTER_OUT_EGR_LOOP` (logical table 5, OF table 45; the token is verified in the branch-22.03 `PIPELINE_STAGES` enum). The REGBIT is defined in `include/ovn/logical-fields.h` as the macro `REGBIT_EGRESS_LOOPBACK "reg9[0]"`. The `next(pipeline=...)` action is parsed in `lib/actions.c` by `parse_NEXT`. Cross-dispatch resolution: `controller/lflow.c` applies an `OFTABLE_LOG_INGRESS_PIPELINE` resubmit.

**Real-world incidents and common failures.** Q4 2024, cluster with 30 tenant LRs and floating IP hairpins: VM-to-VM traffic within a tenant through a FIP occasionally went through two pipeline rounds, adding about 200 microseconds of latency. Diagnose: `ovn-trace` showed `reg9[0]` flipping incorrectly between 1 and 0 due to a priority class conflict after upgrading OVN from 22.03 to 23.06. Mitigation: rebuild lflow with `ovn-nbctl --wait=hv sync` and verify that priority 1000 catches the loopback exit. Common pitfall: policy routing accidentally creates a loop, the packet passes through stage 45 multiple times with `reg9[0]=1`, priority 1000 lets it through, and the next ingress stage redirects it again, eventually dropping at TTL 0. Fix: simplify the policy routing and use `ovn-trace` to inspect each round.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables `-m conntrack --ctstate DNAT -j MARK ... ; -j MASQUERADE` for hairpin is analogous (per host). VMware NSX-T hairpin vNIC is analogous (per edge gateway). The OVN distinction: a dedicated REGBIT in the logical pipeline enables declarative cross-pipeline dispatch; the gateway chassis re-injects without an extra tunnel encapsulation; the semantics clearly distinguish "loop prevention" from "intentional re-injection" through priority classes.

---

## Secondary and peripheral stages (see Part 13.16 for the full table ID list)


### LS_IN secondary (about 10 stages)

`LS_IN_LB_AFF_*`, `LS_IN_PRE_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_NAT_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP`, `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE`.

### LS_IN peripheral (about 12 stages)

`LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP`, `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND`, `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB`, `LS_IN_PUT_FDB`, `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL`, `LS_IN_LB_AFF_LEARN`, and so on.

### LS_OUT (11 stages)

`LS_OUT_PRE_LB`, `LS_OUT_ACL`, `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2`, and so on.

### LR_IN secondary (9 stages)

`LR_IN_DEFRAG`, `LR_IN_UNSNAT`, `LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS`, `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`, `LEARN_NEIGHBOR`, `IP_ROUTING`, `IP_ROUTING_ECMP`, `POLICY`, `POLICY_ECMP`. (`LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE` already has a full treatment at §13.19.41; `LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST` at §13.19.42.)

### LR_OUT (6 stages)

`LR_OUT_POST_UNDNAT`, `LR_OUT_SNAT`, and so on. (`LR_OUT_UNDNAT` already has a full treatment at §13.19.43; `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` at §13.19.44; `LR_OUT_EGR_LOOP` (egress loopback) at §13.19.45.)

## §13.19.X Quick reference for peripheral stages

> The groups below summarise the remaining 38 secondary and peripheral stages; the full treatment lives in Part 13.16 (the table ID map) and the related subparts (Part 13.3 for ACL, LB, and NAT; Part 13.10 for DHCP; Part 18.0 for the ARP responder; Part 19.0 for PMTUD; and so on).

### LS secondary group (10 stages)

> Five stages in this group (`LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP`, `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE`, `LS_OUT_PRE_LB`, `LS_OUT_ACL`, `LS_OUT_PORT_SEC_L2`) already have a full treatment in §13.19.21 through §13.19.25 above. The remaining stages (`LS_IN_LB_AFF_CHECK`, `LS_IN_LB_AFF_LEARN`, `LS_IN_PRE_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_NAT_HAIRPIN`, `LS_IN_HAIRPIN`) have full treatment in §13.19.11 through §13.19.15.

**Importance.** Medium. Specialised pipeline functionality (LB affinity, hairpin SNAT, ARP responder, egress ACL, egress port security L2).

**Version differences.** Most stages were introduced in the OVN 21.06 LB affinity and hairpin overhaul. 22.03 is stable.

**Real-world incident.** In production: LB affinity sticky-session debugging through `ovn-trace` stage observation; `ARP_ND_RSP` local short-circuit (which avoids broadcast).

**Synthetic exercise.** `ovn-trace` with a stateful packet to observe the stage transitions.

**Comparison with other systems.** Cisco LB sticky-session is analogous (LB_AFF). Linux bridge proxy ARP is analogous (`ARP_ND_RSP`).

> The LR secondary group (the five stages `LR_IN_DEFRAG`, `LR_IN_UNSNAT`, `LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS`, `LR_IN_ND_RA_OPTIONS`, `LR_OUT_SNAT`) already has a full treatment in §13.19.16 through §13.19.20 above.

### LS peripheral group (12 stages)

Stages: `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_IP`, `LS_IN_PORT_SEC_ND`, `LS_IN_LOOKUP_FDB`, `LS_IN_PUT_FDB`, `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL`, `LS_IN_LB_AFF_LEARN`, `LS_OUT_PRE_ACL`, `LS_OUT_LB_AFF_CHECK`, `LS_OUT_PRE_STATEFUL`, `LS_OUT_LB`, and so on.

**Importance.** Peripheral. Specialised stages.

**Version differences.** Stable across versions, with only minor additions.

**Synthetic exercise.** `ovn-trace` stage observation only.

**Comparison with other systems.** Per-stage analogues vary; most are OVN-specific abstractions.

### LR peripheral group (11 stages)

Stages: `LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR`, `LR_IN_LEARN_NEIGHBOR`, `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING`, `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING_ECMP`, `LR_IN_POLICY`, `LR_IN_POLICY_ECMP`, `LR_OUT_POST_UNDNAT`, and so on. (Five stages in this group, namely `LR_IN_ARP_RESOLVE`, `LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST`, `LR_OUT_UNDNAT`, `LR_OUT_DELIVERY`, and `LR_OUT_EGR_LOOP`/egress loopback, already have a full treatment in §13.19.41 through §13.19.45.)

**Importance.** Peripheral. ECMP routing, policy-based routing, and neighbor resolution.

**Version differences.** ECMP from OVN 22.06 onwards. Policy from OVN 21.06 onwards. Stable since.

**Real-world incident.** The ECMP asymmetric routing case (cited in REF Section 4 Scenario 10).

**Synthetic exercise.** Run `ovn-nbctl lr-route-add lr-main 0.0.0.0/0 192.168.1.1`, then exercise an ECMP `lr-route-add` with the `--ecmp` flag.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iproute2 `ip route add ... nexthop` ECMP is analogous. Cisco ECMP CEF is similar.
